Chapter seventy-eight on expelling the placenta
If the placenta is retained after childbirth, you should instruct the patient to hold her breath, then make her sneeze with pellitory, and tightly cover her mouth and nostrils. If it comes out with this, fine; otherwise, take a pot and pierce a hole in its lid. Place in it herbs that open the cervix, such as rue, oregano, dill, chamomile, wormwood, savin, and centaury. Use all these medicines or some of them, submerge them in water, and put the pot on the fire. Then place a silver tube over the hole in the pot's lid, with the other end in the cervix, and hold it until the steam reaches the bottom of the uterus. Then make her sneeze as we said, and the placenta will come out quickly.
If it remains after this treatment and does not come out, instruct the midwife to dip her left hand in rose oil or marshmallow extract, then insert it into the vagina and search for the placenta. If she finds it, she should grasp it and pull it gently, little by little, until it comes out.
If it is adhered deep in the uterus, insert the hand as we described until you find the placenta. Then pull it gently, little by little, taking care lest the uterus prolapses with strong pulling. Rather, it should be moved gently to the sides, right and left, then increase the pulling force; it will then respond and detach from the adhesion.
If the cervix is closed, we have described the treatment with sneezing and the treatment with the pot and herbs.
If it does not come out with all that we have described, beware of being rough with her in pulling again. But you should tie what has come out of it, at the cervix, to the woman's thigh, then inject her with the quadruple ointment. It will rot it after a few days, and it will dissolve and come out. However, if it rots, a foul odor will rise from it to the stomach and head, and this will harm the patient. So, it is necessary to use suitable fumigation for this, and some of the ancients have tried fumigation with garden cress and dried figs.
Picture of the instrument with which a woman is fumigated in case of retention of menses, placenta, and the like:
It is made of lead, resembling a funnel. او تصنع من نحاس
The thin end is placed in the vagina. والطرف الواسع على النار
You hold it until that incense is gone. وتعيد غيره
Chapter seventy-nine on the treatment of unperforated hemorrhoids.
Many boys may be born with their anuses not perforated, having been blocked by a thin membrane. فينبغى للقابلة أن تثقب ذلك الصفاق بأصبعها.
Then she should put on it cotton dipped in wine and oil. ثمّ تعالجه بالمراهم حتّى يبرأ،
If you fear that it will close up, place a lead tube in the opening for many days and remove it when the child needs to defecate.
Blockage of the anus may also occur from the healing of a wound or a swelling. فينبغى أن يشقّ ذلك الاندمال ثمّ يعالج بما ذكرنا من العلاج والرصا ص،
Chapter eighty on the treatment of fistulas that occur in the lower part (of the body).
Fistulas that occur in the lower part of the body are a thickening and hardening that occurs near the anus from the outside or in the perineum from one of the sides, and the fistula may be single or multiple.
If this thickening becomes chronic, it opens and a watery white fluid or thin pus flows from it, and some of these fistulas may penetrate to the anus and to the intestine, and some may not be penetrating.
The penetrating ones can be recognized by the feces and gas that come out of them when the patient defecates, and sometimes worms come out of them.
And some of these fistulas, if they are in the perineum, may penetrate to the bladder and to the urethra, and some of them may penetrate to the hip joint and to the coccyx.
And what distinguishes a fistula penetrating to the anus from a non-penetrating one is that you insert your index finger into the anus and insert a thin probe into the fistula, made of copper or iron if the fistula is not curved. If it is curved, then insert a thin lead probe or a horsehair into it until you feel the probe or the hair in your finger.
If you do not feel it at all, and nothing of feces, gas, or worms comes out of the hole, as we have said, then know that it is not penetrating, so proceed with the treatment.
And if the fistula penetrates to the bladder or to the urethra, the evidence for this is the exit of urine from it and its resistance to healing with medicines.
And if it penetrates to the hip joint or to the coccyx, the sign of this is the arrival of the probe there, if there is no curvature to the bone, and the presence of pain in that area, and the constant discharge of pus from it, and that no treatment is effective on it, and it does not heal with ointment.
All these penetrating fistulas are incurable, and their treatment is a futile effort for those ignorant doctors who attempt to treat them.
As for those that are not penetrating and not chronic, there is hope for their healing with iron, as I will describe and as I have experienced.
This is that you lay the patient before you on his back and raise his legs up and his thighs inclined towards his abdomen, then you insert the copper or lead probe if there is curvature in the fistula until you know where the probe ends. If the patient feels it towards the anus, then you should insert your index finger into the anus. If you feel in your finger that the probe has penetrated by itself, exposed, without feeling any peritoneum or flesh between your finger and it, then know for certain that it is penetrating, so do not bother with it, for it is incurable, as we have said.
And they have said that it heals in some people rarely.
And among the treatments that are hoped to be beneficial is that you heat a thin cautery iron according to the width of the fistula, as mentioned before, and insert it hot into the fistula until it reaches near the anus, then repeat it two or three times until you know that all the excess thickened flesh that resembles the tube of a bird's feather has been burned. Then treat it with wicks soaked in fat until the burned flesh comes out, then treat it with healing ointments. If it heals, then good, otherwise it will never heal with any other treatment.
But if you insert the probe and it does not penetrate to your finger that is in the anus, and there is a thick barrier of flesh or peritoneum between it and the probe, and you see the fistula next to the surface of the skin, then incise the skin from the beginning of the fistula while you pass through the incision with the probe while it is in the fistula until you reach the incision where the tip of the probe ends, and the probe is released and falls out.
Then clean out the thickened flesh that resembles the tube of a bird's feather, leaving nothing of it, then treat it with healing ointments until it heals.
If the bleeding overcomes you and prevents you from cutting that flesh, then the best thing you can do and the quickest benefit is to cauterize it with fire or with a strong medicine, because cauterization with fire combines two good things: burning that excess flesh, stopping the bleeding, and drying the moisture. Then treat it with wicks soaked in fat or powdered sulfur with oil until the area suppurates and the burned flesh comes out in the pus.
Then treat the wound with healing ointments that generate solid flesh, which are medicines that have astringency and dryness. For if it heals, it is cured, and do not fear recurrence.
If the fistula has reached the depth of the anus and is far from the surface of the body, insert your finger into the anus and search with it. If you feel the probe and there is a barrier of peritoneum or flesh between you and it, and it is close to penetrating, then the action in it is only by way of hope and expectation, and that is to use one of three methods in it:
Either cauterize it as we have said,
or incise it until you reach its bottom to be able to insert the wicks and treatment closely, and do not go too far with the incision so as not to cut the muscle surrounding the anus, causing the patient to have involuntary defecation. Then treat it with what we have mentioned, and perhaps it will heal, as we have said,
or penetrate that barrier and pierce it after incising it near the anus, as we have said, either with the probe or with another sharp-tipped instrument. Then clean all the thickened flesh in the incision as far as you can thoroughly. Then close the entire wound with the incision and the upper opening until it seals, and leave the fistula open inside the anus, so it will be easier for the patient.
And the fistula may be perforated in this manner: if you insert the probe into the fistula and it is on the side of the anus towards the surface of the body with the skin and the rim of the anus, then take a probe with a perforated tip like a cobbler's needle in this way: وأدخل فيها خيطا مفتولا من خمسة خيوط او نحوها
If it is perforated in the edge of the anus from inside nearby, then bring the thread out of that hole by inserting your finger into the anus and bring out the end of the thread and gather both ends together and tighten them and leave it for a day or two.
Whenever the thread cuts into the flesh, tighten it well until the flesh between the ends of the threads is cut and falls off.
Then treat the wound until it heals and recovers.
If the opening of the fistula is not perforated, then perforate it however you can, unless it is very deep, then you should not do that because of the muscle, lest you cut it.
Then do with it as we mentioned until it heals.
The image of the hooked scalpel with which fistulas are incised, its curvature is very sharp and the other side is not sharp so that you do not cut with it what is not needed, as you see.
Chapter eighty-one on the perforation of hemorrhoids from which blood flows, their excision, and the treatment of fissures.
Hemorrhoids are of two types: either they are inside the anus, resembling red blisters, or like grapes, and they are small and large, and blood flows from them constantly, and they are single and multiple, and they are outside the anus and on its edges, except that those that are outside the anus are mostly dry, with yellow water or a little blood flowing constantly from them, and they are the color of the skin.
And the treatment of what is inside the anus is that you order the patient to defecate and strain until the anus comes out and the warts appear to you, then you quickly hook them with hooks or hold them with your fingernail, then cut them at their roots.
If the hooks do not hold in them because of their moisture and looseness, then scrape them with a rough cloth or a piece of rough bread or pull them with your fingers, then cut them, then sprinkle on them after cutting some sharp powders so that it takes the place of cauterization or cauterize them as mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, then treat them with the rest of the treatment until they heal.
If the anus does not respond to coming out, then inject the patient with an enema that has a little sting to wash what is in the anus and it will come out quickly when the patient strains.
As for the warts outside the anus, their matter is easy and simple, which is that you take them with your fingernail or hook them with a hook and cut them, then treat them with what we mentioned until they heal.
And whoever dislikes cutting with iron, it is appropriate to use piercing them in this way: take a twisted thread and insert it into a needle, then pull the wart up and pierce it with the needle at its root from the other side, and wrap the ends of the thread under the needle while it is crossed, and tighten the wart tightly, then tie the thread and remove the needle.
Do this with all the warts and leave one of them unpierced so that the excess blood flows from it.
Then put on the anus a cloth dipped in rose oil or cerotti and put it on the anus and order the patient to rest, then leave it until they fall, and if the warts fall, treat them with ointments and the rest of the treatment until they heal.
As for the treatment of fissures, it often occurs from dryness of the stool and constipation, so if it becomes chronic and no medicine works on it, it is appropriate to scrape it with a scalpel blade or your fingernail until it becomes moist and the upper crust that prevents it from healing is removed, then treat it until it heals as it should.
If it does not heal, then repeat scraping it more severely than the first time until it bleeds well and peels, then treat it, and it will heal.
Chapter eighty-two on the treatment of ingrown and non-ingrown nails, dry warts, and corns.
A corn is a round knot, the color of the body, resembling the head of a nail, that forms throughout the body, especially on the soles of the feet and the toes, and causes pain when walking.
It should be incised around the corn and grasped with tweezers or a hook and removed from its root, then treated.
And if you wish, cauterize it as described previously in its chapter.
As for dry warts, each one is a small, rough, dense, round protuberance on the surface of the body, and frequently occurs on the hands of children.
Its treatment is to stretch the wart and cut it off, or pierce it with a silk thread or a hair until it falls off. وإن شئت كويته على ما تقدّم بالنار او بالدواء الحادّ،
Inverted and non-inverted warts can be removed with the instrument that I mentioned in the treatment of moles.
As for a mole, it is also a small, dense, thick protuberance on the surface of the body, going very deep, and if cold affects it, one feels in it something similar to the sting of ants, and it occurs throughout the body, mostly on the hands.
Its treatment is to take a tube from a goose feather or a strong eagle feather, and place that tube on the mole until the circle of the tube surrounds it from all sides, then rotate your hand with the tube until you incise around the mole and make it go deep into the flesh, and the mole will be removed from its root.
And if you wish, you can do that with a copper or iron tube in this manner.
The top of the tube to the thin part should be solid and twisted to make it easy for the fingers to hold and twist it.
And if you wish, cauterize the fistula as described previously in the cauterization of warts in the chapter on cauterization.
Chapter eighty-three on images of the instruments that use cupping glasses in diseases of the anus, diarrhea, and colic.
The syringe may be made of silver, or of Chinese porcelain, or of cast or hammered copper.
And this instrument can be made small and large, according to those who use it. فتكون التى تستعمل فى علاج الصبيان الصغار صغارا
And those whose anuses are narrow or painful, their syringes should be very gentle.
Picture of a large syringe:
Its upper funnel, where the wineskin is attached, should be wide in this manner. له حاجز حيث يربط الزقّ كما ترى.
And its lower end, which enters the anus, should be solid, very smooth, and thin; on one of its sides are two holes and on the other side is one hole. تكون سعة الثقب على غلظ المرود او أغلظ قليلا.
And the wineskin, in which you put the medications, should be made from an animal bladder or from thin sheepskin, made in the shape of a small round tablecloth, its diameter a span, pierced with many holes all around.
And the distance between each hole should be the width of a finger, then you insert into those holes a strong thread of two-ply yarn of ten strands or so. وتجمع به الزقّ كالسفرة فإذا وضع فيه الدواء شدّ فى رأس المحقن بين الحجرين شدّا وثيقا ثمّ تحقن به،
And this is a picture of the circle of the wineskin and the hole:
The method of enema is to fill the leather or bladder with the medication, warmed to a degree the patient can tolerate. واحذر أن يكون حارّا جدّا او باردا جدّا
Then the patient lies on his back and raises his legs up, and a mat or blanket is placed under him.
Then the tip of the enema syringe is lubricated with oil, or egg white, or fenugreek mucilage, or similar. ثمّ تدخله فى المقعدة برفق.
Then the syringe is removed, and the patient raises his legs against the wall. If the medication stimulates him to defecate, he should hold it as long as he can. وإن نام به الليل كلّه كان أبلغ فى المنغعة، فإن حقن به فى علل الإسهال واستعجل خروجه فلتعد الحقنة حتّى يبرأ.
If there are hemorrhoids or painful swellings in the anus, the syringe should be very smooth, smoother than the syringes used for children. This is a picture of a smooth syringe with its leather tightly stretched:
Chapter eighty-four on the treatment of wounds/injuries/surgeries.
I have mentioned in the division of the treatment of what resembles cauterizations, and I mention here their treatment by hand and with medicines to perfection, so I say that wounds vary according to the thing that causes the wound and according to the place where the wound occurs, so the things that cause wounds are many, such as a blow from a stone or a cut from a sword or a knife, or a stab from a spear, a stick, or an arrow, and so on from many things.
As for the wounds according to the places of the body, they are like the wound that occurs on the head, neck, chest, abdomen, liver, and other organs.
And I am describing the treatment of some wounds to make them a measurement and a law for all other wounds, and I begin with simple head wounds, especially because the complex ones will be mentioned at the beginning of the third chapter of this book.
So I say that whenever a simple wound occurs in the head, and there is no bone fracture, you should look, and if it is from a blow from a stone or the like, and it has only torn the skin, and the wound is large, and you fear the occurrence of a hot swelling on the patient, then hasten and bleed him on the spot, and do not delay that, and draw blood from him according to his strength if there is no impediment to bleeding, especially if the blood that comes out of the wound is slight, and let the bleeding be from the opposite side of the wounded area, and warn the patient against fullness, drink, and coarse foods.
If there is an impediment to bleeding him, let him reduce his food, and not approach meat or drinks.
And apply to the wound, if a hot swelling occurs in it, cotton dipped in rose oil alone or with the drink that has astringency.
And if you are safe from the hot swelling, apply to the wound, if it is fresh with its blood and the air has not changed it, the powder that I will describe to you shortly.
But if the air has changed it, apply some ointments to it until it draws pus, then treat it until it heals.
If the wound is large and is from a sword cut or the like, and its edges do not meet with the bandages, then join it with sutures as I describe in suturing abdominal wounds.
If the skin has exposed the bone and is hanging, and its hold is only in a small attachment, cut it off, then treat the wound with medicines that repair solid flesh in it instead of the skin, and order the patient to eat foods that have firmness, such as heads, porridges, and the like.
If putrefaction occurs in the skin and it does not stick to the bone, cut off all the putrefied part and discard it, then treat it.
If in these wounds there is an artery or a vein bleeding from it, and the blood has not stopped with the medicines, then search for the artery, and if you find it and it is not severed, then sever it with the scalpel and tie it, and if necessary, if what we mentioned does not help, cauterize it until the blood stops, and if these wounds are small and simple, then their matter is easy and simple, it is sufficient to treat them by sprinkling this powder on them while they are still bleeding before the air changes them.
The recipe for the powder is that one part of frankincense is taken, two parts of mastic, and three parts of slaked or unslaked lime, all are crushed and sifted, and the wound is packed with it, and it is tied tightly until it adheres to it well and clots with the blood, then you tie it from above with bandages and leave it, do not loosen it as long as it does not swell, for the medicine will not come off it until it heals.
And that lime alone may do it if you do not have frankincense and mastic, and it may also do this action by itself in many large wounds if its placement on it and its tightening are mastered, and the wounds are fresh with their blood as long as the air has not changed them.
But if the air has changed it somewhat, especially if it is in the summer time, then there is no meaning in putting this powder, so treat it as we mentioned, by applying some ripening ointments to it, or applying a porridge of barley flour made with water and honey until it draws pus, then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
But if a bone fracture occurs with the wound, and it is slight, then pull it with the forceps.
I have mentioned the treatment of head fractures in what is to be resumed.
In neck surgery,
If the wound occurs in the neck, there is no difference in treatment between it and a head wound if it is simple, but if it has cut a nerve or an artery from the neck, and if it is a nerve, there is no way but to avoid treating it with what constricts severely, such as verdigris, vitriol, and the like, because they harm the nerve and cause it to spasm, and nothing cold should be placed on the wound at all, because the essence of the nerve is cold and its connection to the brain, which is the noblest of organs, and what you treat it with should be of emollient medicines such as quicklime washed with fresh water several times, moistened with oil or with rose oil, and tutty if it is also washed with fresh water, and all other mineral stones in this manner, and moist ointments.
But if the wound is large, use sutures or join its edges with bandages, then treat it until it heals.
If the wound has depth and a pocket has occurred in its bottom where pus has collected, then drain it in the lowest place in it.
If an artery has been cut in the wound and you find its blood bleeding, then sever it or tie it or cauterize it if necessary.
If the wound has cut some or all of the cartilage of the larynx, and the jugular veins are safe, then join the edges of the skin with sutures over the trachea, and do not touch the larynx, but straighten it and return it to its natural shape, then tighten it tightly and leave it for days, then treat it with ointments that have astringency and drying until it heals.
If the wound has affected the bone, then examine it, and if there are bone fragments in it, then extract them all or what is possible of them, and what you cannot, leave it until the wound putrefies and heals and its removal becomes easy.
If the wound is fresh, then sprinkle on it the powder that we described, and if bone putrefies in the wound and becomes a fistula, then treat it as will be mentioned in its chapter.
In surgery of the chest and between the shoulders,
If it is a stab wound from a spear or a knife, and you see that it has depth, then look, and if air comes out of it when the patient breathes, then know that it is a fatal wound, and if it has no depth and is fresh with its blood, then do not put the powder in it at first glance, and do not tighten it so that the blood does not get trapped in its depth and recoil to the heart and kill the patient, but put in it an attractive ointment and loosen it twice a day, and if you do not have an ointment, then put worn cotton in the mouth of the wound to absorb what comes out of it of moisture, and let the patient sleep on the wound so that what collects in it flows out.
And if three days or more have passed for the wound, and the patient has not experienced any convulsions, bad palpitations, or shortness of breath, and you see that all his other conditions are good, then know that the wound is safe, so treat it with bandaging and the rest of the treatment until it heals.
If its healing is difficult and it constantly suppurates, then know that it has become a fistula, so treat it according to its chapter.
And if time has passed for the wound of the chest or back, treat it with what has been mentioned of suturing if it is large, or with the powder if it is small.
If it has affected the bone and cut off fragments from it, then examine the wound and hasten to remove those fragments if they are free, and if they are not free, then leave them until the wound putrefies, for it will make their removal easier.
As for the rest of the wounds occurring in the rest of the organs, their rule in treatment is the rule of what we have mentioned.
Chapter eighty-five on wounds of the abdomen, protrusion of the intestines, and their suturing.
The hernia that occurs in the abdomen can be large, small, or medium. From a large one, an intestine or several intestines may protrude, making their insertion and suturing more difficult and laborious.
The small hernia can also be difficult in another way, so its reduction is necessarily more difficult, and if the intestine is not immediately returned to its place, it swells and thickens, making its insertion difficult. For this reason, the medium hernia is the best, because the reduction of the intestine is not as difficult as in these two types.
Know that the suturing of the abdomen is of four types: two common sutures are suitable for suturing the abdomen and for suturing all other wounds of the body, and the other two are specific sutures that apply to all other wounds, except that they are more specific to suturing the abdomen, and I will describe them to you one by one with explanation and clarification.
So I say that if the wound is small and something of the intestine comes out and its reduction is difficult, it is for one of two reasons: either because of the smallness of the hernia, as we said, or because the intestine has been inflated by the cold air. If this is the case, it should be warmed by immersing a sponge or damp cloth in lukewarm water alone or in which has been cooked zedoary, costus, and spikenard, and dabbing the intestine with it until the swelling subsides. This can also be done with a drink that has astringency, which is better than water alone in resolving the swelling.
When you see that the swelling has subsided, wet the intestine with water in which marsh mallow or hollyhock has been cooked, for this will facilitate its entry with the easiest effort.
If its return is difficult after this treatment, then make a small incision in the hernia with the instrument used to incise fistulas. The first treatment, if possible, is better than the incision, and one is only compelled to make the incision after necessity and the failure of other means.
This is a picture of the instrument:
Its one curved side is limited, its other side is unlimited, and the thin end is not as thin as a scalpel but rather slightly blunt, and it is a tool that resembles a small probe, as you see.
So, if the wound widens and the intestine enters, it should be returned to its natural shape and proper place if you are able to do so, for that is best.
However, if the rupture is wide and is in the lower abdomen, the patient should lie on his back and place his legs higher than his head.
And if it is in the upper abdomen, he should place his head and chest higher than his lower body, and likewise, if the rupture is in one of the two sides of the abdomen, always make your intention and purpose to have the side where the surgery is higher than the other side.
And thus, it should be used in major surgeries and in medium surgeries.
But in small surgeries, you place it according to what is possible for you.
Then you bring before you a gentle servant who holds the entire rupture with his hands and brings its edges together, then reveals it to the one in charge of stitching bit by bit.
And this is the description of the single common stitch, which is that you take a needle or several needles according to the size of the wound, then leave from the edge of the rupture a space the thickness of the little finger, and insert a single needle without threading it into the edges of the skin along with the edges of the peritoneum that is under the skin from the inside until you pass it through from that side, having brought together the edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneum, making four layers.
Then you tighten with a doubled thread around the needle several times from both sides until the edges of the wound are brought together tightly.
Then you also leave a space the thickness of a finger and insert another needle, then attach it to the thread as you did with the first needle, and continue doing this with the needles you need until you finish stitching the entire wound.
And let the needles be medium between thick and thin, because very thin needles quickly cut the flesh, and thick ones are also difficult to insert into the skin, therefore, they should be medium in thinness and thickness.
And it is not appropriate to insert the needle into the edge of the skin too close, lest the flesh quickly tear and the wound open before it heals, nor should you stitch too far apart, lest the wound be prevented from healing.
Then you cut the ends of the needles so that they do not harm the patient while he sleeps, and you make for him supports from pieces of linen on all sides, which hold the ends of the needles, and you leave them until you know that the wound has healed.
This type of stitching with needles is thus more suitable in small surgeries, because it may be sufficient to stitch them with one needle or two or so.
As for the description of the second common stitch, it is that you gather with the stitching the four edges, I mean the two edges of the skin and the two edges of the peritoneum, at once with a needle containing a twisted thread, moderate in thinness and thickness.
Then, when you have passed the needle through these four edges, you return the needle from the side from which you began, so that the thread lies intertwined from the top of the wound, so that the stitching is like the stitching of the small sacks with which goods are tied, and you make between each stitch and stitch a distance the thickness of the little finger, and this stitching is known by all people.
With this stitching, I stitched a surgery that occurred to a man in his abdomen, who had been wounded with a knife, and the rupture of the surgery was more than a span, and about two spans of his middle intestine had come out, and the rupture was in the middle of the abdomen, so I returned it after his intestine had remained outside the wound for twenty-four hours, and the wound healed in about fifteen days, and I treated him until he recovered, and he lived after that many years, managing all his affairs, and the doctors had judged that he would never recover.
And it is surprising that I did not treat him with an ointment, because I was in a place where there was nothing of the medicines, so I would put worn cotton on the wound twice a day and I would wash it with honey water until it healed.
The summary of the description of this stitching is that you stitch like furriers stitch furs, by stitching the peritoneum once from one side with the edge of the skin, and leaving it once from the other side until you finish.
As for the second type of special stitching that Galen also mentioned, these are his words verbatim, which is that you stitch it like some of the healers stitch it, by bringing together each part to its similar counterpart naturally, so they join the edge of the peritoneum to its other edge and the edge of the peritoneum to its other edge, and that is as I describe to you.
It is appropriate that you insert the needle into the edge of the peritoneum closest to you from the outside and pass it through to the inside in it alone and leave the two edges of the peritoneum, then return the needle and pass it from outside to inside in both edges of the peritoneum, then return it again and pass it from inside to outside in the other edge of the peritoneum that is on the opposite side.
And this type of stitching is better than the easy common stitching, which is the stitching that was mentioned previously.
These are Galen's words verbatim.
And some have said that stitching the abdomen may be straightened with two needles, which is that you insert a single thread in them, and you begin by inserting the needle from your side and passing it through to the other side, and you insert the other needle from the opposite side, so you pass it through from your side, like the stitching of bishops, alike.
And know that if the rupture is in the middle of the abdomen, its stitching is more difficult than other places of the abdomen.
As for the treatment of the wound, its rule in its treatment is the rule of all other surgeries, which is that if you find the wound fresh with its blood before the air changes it, and you have returned the intestine and stitched it and tightened it, then apply to it the healing powders. But if the air has changed the wound, then apply to it some of the ointments that you have prepared until it suppurates and the threads fall out and the peritoneum heals, then treat it as you treat all other surgeries until it heals.
But if you do not have medicines, apply worn cotton to it from the time it begins to suppurate and change it twice a day, as I have informed you, until it heals, for you will not need another treatment in most cases if the surgeries are simple.
If you fear that the major organs will share in the pain of the wound, it is appropriate that you dip soft wool in moderately warm oil or in rose oil and place it around the areas between the groin and the armpit.
If he feels pain or putrefaction in his intestines, which often occurs, then inject him with a lukewarm black astringent syrup, especially if the putrefaction has reached the intestine and has become a wound penetrating its cavity.
And know that whatever of the intestines is thick is easier to heal, and whatever of them is thin is more difficult to heal.
As for the intestine known as the jejunum, it does not accept healing from any surgery that occurs in it at all, because of the abundance of veins in it and their size, and the thinness of its substance and its proximity to the nature of the nerve.
But if what protruded from the wound is the omentum, and you find it fresh, then return it just as you returned the intestine.
But if some time has passed and it has become green or black, it is appropriate that you tie it with a thread above the place where it has blackened, lest bleeding occur, for there are veins and arteries in the omentum.
Then you cut what is below that ligature and make the ends of the thread hanging from below the surgery, outside of it, so that it is easy for you to pull it and remove it when the omentum falls off and the wound suppurates.
Mention of the wound that occurs in the intestine.
But if a small rupture occurs in the intestine, it may be repaired in some people, because I saw a man who had been wounded in his abdomen by a spear thrust, and the wound was to the right of the stomach, and the wound became chronic and became a fistula from which feces and wind came out, so I began to treat him although I did not hope for his recovery, but I continued to treat him gently until he recovered and the place healed.
When I saw that the place had healed, I feared that something bad would happen to the patient in his abdomen, but nothing bad happened to him at all, and he remained in the best of his conditions, healthy, eating, drinking, having intercourse, entering the bath, and exercising in his service.
And some of the experienced have mentioned that whenever a small wound occurs in the intestine, it should be stitched in this manner, which is that large-headed ants are taken, then the edges of the wound are brought together, then an ant with its mouth open is placed on the edges of the wound, and when it grips it and tightens its mouth, its head is cut off, for it will stick and not come undone.
Then another ant is placed near the first, and you continue doing this with several ants according to the size of the wound, then you return it and stitch the wound, for those heads will remain stuck in the intestine until the intestine heals and recovers, and no harm will occur to the patient at all.
It may also be possible to stitch the intestine with the thin thread that is pulled from the intestine of the animal attached to it after it is inserted into a needle, which is that the end of this thread is taken from the intestine and pulled gently, then a thin twisted linen thread is tied to its end, then that thread is inserted into the needle with the intestine's thread in it, and the intestine is stitched with it, then returned to the cavity.
And this type of stitching with ants and intestine is only by way of hope and expectation.
But if the rupture is large and wide, especially if it is in one of the thin intestines, there is no remedy for it and no healing from it at all.
Chapter eighty-six on the treatment of colds and fistulas.
Know that every wound or swelling, if it becomes chronic and old, and becomes an ulcer, and does not heal, and constantly suppurates without ceasing, is called in general, in any organ it may be, a fistula, and we call it zakam.
And the fistula is in reality a hard, white, solidified knot without pain, having a cavity like the cavity of a bird's feather, and therefore some have called it a feather.
And it is sometimes moist, constantly suppurating, and perhaps the moisture ceases at times, and this moisture may be abundant or little, thick or thin.
And I have already presented in the section on wounds that every ulcer that does not heal and in which no flesh grows is due to one of nine causes, one of which is either due to a lack of blood in the body, or to its poor quality, or because there is hard flesh inside it and on its lips that prevents the growth of good flesh, or because it is full of damage and dirt, or because the ulcer itself is putrid and the matter that suppurates it is of poor quality, or because the medicine is not suitable for its treatment, or because of corruption that has occurred in the town, such as a plague, or because of a peculiarity in the town, as happened in the city of Zaragoza, where the maturation of diseases is difficult and the healing of swellings is slow by nature, or because there is one bone or several bones in it.
And I have explained all these causes, their signs, and their treatments with medicines in the section, and it is necessary for me here to acquaint you with the treatment of fistula and zakam, which is by way of manual work.
Know that fistulas can occur in all parts of the body, and among them are fistulas that end in large veins and arteries, or in a nerve, or in the peritoneum, or in the intestines, or in the bladder, or in a rib, or in a vertebra of the back, or in a joint of the complex joints, such as the hand and foot, and among them are those that lead to the vicinity of a vital organ, and so on.
So whatever of these fistulas are of this description, they are among the diseases that are difficult to heal, which do not accept treatment, and working on them is hardship and ignorance.
So if there are fistulas that do not reach these places that I have mentioned, and you hope and desire to treat them, and you want to arrive at the knowledge of that, then take a probe of copper or iron if the fistula passes straight, and explore it with it.
But if there is a curvature in the fistula, then explore it with a probe of thin lead, because the lead softens its body, so it slips in easily and bends towards the curvature.
And if the fistula has many openings that you cannot trace with the probe, then inject one of its openings, for the moisture that you inject into it will pass towards the other openings and flow from them, then complete the examination in any way possible for you to know if there is a bone or nerve, or if the bottom of the fistula is far or near, or if it is one fistula with many openings, and ascertain all of that with the extent of your ability by inquiring of the patient about the presence of pain when you press your hand on the place, and so on, from the signs and causes incidental to the swelling.
So if you have ascertained all of that with certainty, then proceed to the treatment with confidence, which is that you look, and if the fistula is apparent and near, or in a healthy place far from a joint, or nerve, or artery, or vein, or one of the places that I have mentioned to you, then incise the fistula according to what I have previously described, and extract what is in it of solidified matter, corrupted flesh, excess flesh, and what grows in its opening of flesh, or wart, and so on, and treat it until it heals.
But if the fistula is deep-bottomed and straight, then you should incise it in depth as much as you can, then cleanse it of all its corrupted flesh, then use threads soaked in sharp medicines and insert them into the bottom of the fistula that you reach with the iron.
Do that to it several times until that sharp medicine eats away all that remains in the bottom of the fistula of corruption, then treat it with ointments that grow healthy flesh until it heals. If it does not heal with that, then cauterize it according to what has been previously described.
And if the cause of the fistula is a bone, and that is confirmed to you, then examine it and incise it as I have told you, unless you are prevented by an obstacle of a vein or nerve or vital organ, as we have said.
And if the bone is exposed to you, and there is some corruption and blackness in it, then scrape it until its corruption is gone, then treat it with what will heal it until it heals.
And if it does not heal and suppurates as it used to do, then know that you have not reached all of the eradication of the corruption, so expose all of it again, and complete its scraping and cleansing with your utmost effort, then dress it.
If it heals, otherwise, know that the corruption is in a deep recess of corruption. Expose all of what is on it again, and fully scrape it and cleanse it with your best effort if the corrupted bone was a small bone and you were able to pull it, pull it with the gentle hooks that are suitable for that. If there were many bones, try to pull them all and don't leave anything from it with your best effort. If something from the ligaments obstructs you before it, and there's no danger in cutting it, then cut those ligaments and the flesh, if it is there, and free the bones.
If you can't extract the bones at your time, then apply to the wound what putrefies it, and leave it for days until what is around those bones of flesh and ligaments putrefies. Keep the wound from healing, the incision that you made during your work. Rather, put on it, if you fear that, cotton dipped in powdered sulfur with oil or cotton dipped in the Egyptian ointment or one of the green ointments. For when those ligaments putrefy and the bones become bare, it will be easy to extract and pull them.
And if it is one large bone, such as the tibia or femur bone and the like, and what is corrupted of it is only its surface, then scrape it thoroughly until that blackness and corruption are gone, then dress the wound.
But if what is corrupted of it is a large part, and the corruption has reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to saw it and cut it all to where the corruption ends, then treat it until it heals.
And I will tell you about a zakam that occurred to a man in his leg, so that you may take it as an example and an aid in your treatment.
And this repetition in his operation and incision was necessary because of the patient's weakness and his low tolerance, and my fear of death for him, because he used to have at all times, from excessive vomiting, a bad faint. So he recovered completely, and hard flesh grew in the place of the bone, and his condition in his body improved, and his strength returned, and he managed his affairs, and he was not afflicted in walking with any harm that would harm him at all.
And if a protruding bone in a place of the body is broken, then it is necessary to saw it in this manner: Take a bandage and tie it to the end of the protruding bone and order someone to pull it upwards, and make another bandage of wool thicker than the first bandage, then tie it to the flesh that is under the bone and pull its end so that the flesh is pulled downwards, and you expose the flesh from the place that you want to saw so that the saw does not harm the flesh, and you place a piece of wood or a board under the bone from below tightly, because if you do that, the saw will not be prevented from cutting the corrupted bone.
And the sawing should be a little above the place of the corruption, so that there is no corruption in the cavity of the bone, so it does not appear on its surface to the senses, and you are forced to saw it again.
And if the bone is corrupted and not protruding, but connected to each other, and the corruption is in its middle or in some of it, then expose the flesh from all its sides, then place the wood from below, then saw it from the first side where the corruption is, and when you have finished sawing it, saw it from the other side, and let the sawing be a little away from the corruption, as we have said.
And if the corruption is in a joint, you cut the corrupted joint itself and saw the bone where it connects from the other side.
And if the corruption is in the connection of two joints, then there is no solution but scraping.
And if the corruption is in the metacarpus of the hand or the metatarsus of the foot, then the matter of its treatment is very difficult, but you should peel the corruption however it appears to you, and scrape it and cleanse it in any way you can, and by any trick that is right for you, as long as you are not obstructed by a vein or nerve.
And know that the cutters and saws for cutting these bones are many, according to the position of the bones, their erection, their thickness and thinness, their largeness and smallness, their hardness and porosity, therefore, it is necessary to prepare for each type of work a tool similar to that work.
And know that the works themselves may guide you to the type of tool that you need if you have long practice and knowledge of the arts of this craft, because whoever is skilled in the craft and has witnessed various types of diseases may deduce for himself similar tools for each disease. And I have illustrated for you at the end of this chapter several tools that you may take as examples to follow and as a measure to compare with others, God willing. صورة منشار:

Chapter eighty-seven on the amputation of limbs and sawing of bones
The extremities may rot, either from an external cause or from an internal cause.
If you treat this corruption with medical treatment and the treatment does not succeed, and you see the corruption spreading in the limb, nothing stopping it, then it is necessary to amputate that limb to the point where the corruption has reached, so that the patient may be saved thereby from death or from a great affliction that is greater than the loss of the limb.
And the sign of whoever is afflicted with this is that the limb blackens until you think that fire has burned it, or it rots after blackening until that rot spreads to what is next to that limb and takes over the whole body, so hasten to amputate it.
And likewise, if the cause of the corruption is from the sting of some vermin, such as a sea scorpion, a viper, a tarantula, and the like.
If the corruption or the sting is at the tip of the finger, then amputate the finger and do not allow the corruption to spread to the hand.
And likewise, if it has taken hold in the hand, then amputate it from the base of the forearm, and do not allow the corruption to spread until it takes hold in both bones of the forearm.
And likewise, if it has taken hold in the forearm, then amputate the forearm at the elbow in the joint itself.
If the corruption has passed and you see that it has taken hold towards the shoulder, then do not amputate the shoulder at all, for in that is the death of the patient, and use other treatment according to the ability.
And likewise you do with the foot if the corruption has taken hold in the toe, then amputate the toe at one of the phalanges.
And if it has taken hold in the metatarsal of the foot, then amputate the entire foot. فإن صعد الى الركبة فاقطع الساق عند مفصل الركبة،
And if the corruption has reached above the knee, then there is no recourse but to leave it and surrender the patient to death.
The method of amputating or dissecting the limb is to tie a bandage at the bottom of the place you want to amputate and tie another bandage above the place, and an assistant stretches one bandage downwards and another assistant stretches the upper bandage upwards, and you strip the flesh between the two bandages with a wide scalpel until all the flesh is exposed, then it is cut or dissected.
And it is necessary to place linen rags on all sides so that the saw does not touch the healthy area, causing the patient additional pain and a hot swelling.
If bleeding occurs during your work, cauterize the area quickly or apply some blood-stopping powders, then return to your treatment until you are finished, then bandage the wounded limb with a suitable bandage and treat it until it heals.
And I will tell you an example of a man who suffered in his leg this very same condition that I am describing to you, and that is that blackness occurred in his leg with a burning sensation resembling fire, and that corruption first occurred in his toe until it took over the entire foot, so the man, on his own initiative, when he saw the corruption spreading in the limb, with the intensity of the pain and burning he was experiencing, amputated it at the joint and recovered.
Then, after a long time had passed, this same corruption appeared in the index finger of his hand, so he came to me and I tried to stop that excess with the medicines I applied to the hand after cleaning his body, but the excess was not deterred and it began to spread to the other finger until the corruption took hold in the hand, so he called on me to amputate his hand, but I refused him, hoping to stop that excess and also fearing death for him if his hand were amputated, because the man's strength was failing.
So when he despaired of me, he returned to his town, and I heard about him that he, on his own initiative, amputated his entire hand and recovered.
I have only told this story to be a help in what occurs of this type of disease and to be a guide to be followed and acted upon.
Chapter eighty-eight on the treatment of fistulas and how to inject them with medications.
If a swelling occurs in some fleshy member and the duration of the swelling is prolonged until it gathers pus, then bursts or is lanced and all the pus within it comes out and the place remains empty like a vessel, and the skin on it, like a rag, has thinned, and its corruption has not reached the point of affecting bone, nerve, or ligament, for this reason it deserves to be called a 'hidden' (mukhaba'), and it is not called a fistula unless its duration lengthens until the corruption affects something from these members, then at that time it is called a fistula or zakama.
And its treatment is to cut that whole skin as it goes around, especially if it has thinned and become like a rag, and you are certain that it will not adhere to the place because of its corruption. But if you hope that the skin will adhere, and the corruption in it has not reached that extent, and there is thickness of flesh in the skin, then treat it with an injection.
And that is, you look at the hidden (mukhaba'), and if it is large and the pus that flows from it has a foul odor, inject it with the Egyptian medicine which is vinegar, oil, honey and verdigris, equal parts, gathered in a vessel, and cooked on the fire until the medicine thickens and comes to the thickness of honey.
Then take your need of it, and thin it with water and honey, and inject it into the hidden (mukhaba') and bind its mouth and leave the medicine in it for about two hours, then remove it by squeezing, do this for days until the hidden (mukhaba') is cleansed and the foul odor goes away.
It may be injected with ash water if you don't have this medicine available.
And that is, you take the ash of vine wood or the ash of oak wood and pour water on it, then filter it and inject it with it until you are certain that the hidden (mukhaba') has been washed.
If the patient cannot tolerate the Egyptian medicine nor the ash water, then inject him with water and honey in which some powdered verdigris has been mixed, or inject him with honey and wine mixed, because it is the nature of honey to cleanse and wash, and wine adheres to the hidden (mukhaba'), especially if the wine has extra astringency and dryness.
So if you do that several times, and you are certain that the corruption of the hidden (mukhaba') has gone, inject it with something that grows flesh in it, such as taking some of the palm ointment and dissolving it with rose oil and astringent wine, or injecting it with some of the other medicines and ointments whose properties we established in the article on ointments.
If the mouth of the hidden (mukhaba') is narrow, not accommodating the tube of the syringe, then widen it slightly with iron or place in it a wick soaked in the Egyptian ointment or the siryqun ointment until it widens. Likewise, if the mouth is too wide, then gather its lips with stitching, leaving of it just enough for the syringe to enter, no more. Likewise, if its mouth from which pus flows is elevated upwards, then slit it in its lowest place so that the pus flows downwards, because if the pus is congested in the depth of the hidden (mukhaba') it prevents flesh from growing in it. If you cannot slit the hidden (mukhaba') towards its bottom as you want, then you must position the limb so that the pus flows easily from it according to what is possible for you, and be sure that no pus is retained in it at all.
As for the healing medicines that are placed on the hidden (mukhaba') externally and how to place them on it, it is that you take one of the healing ointments appropriate to what you want and spread it on a linen cloth, and let the cloth be as large as will cover the entire hidden (mukhaba'). Then cut with scissors opposite the mouth of the hidden (mukhaba') a hole slightly wider than the mouth of the hidden (mukhaba'). Then spread some of the soft ointments on another cloth the size of the hole and place it on it, placed away from the mouth of the hidden (mukhaba') to facilitate the exit of pus from it, and do not remove the large cloth except after several days.
As for the one on the mouth of the hidden (mukhaba'), it is the one that you remove every time, so that the pus gathered in it comes out, and so you know what flows from it of pus, whether it is much or little, or mature or immature.
And with this also, inspect the site of the hidden (mukhaba') itself, whether its owner feels pain in it or there is no pain in it, and whether there is swelling in it or there is no swelling in it.
So if your treatment proceeds in this way, and you see that the substance that comes out is slight on the mouth of the hidden (mukhaba'), then know that the hidden (mukhaba') has adhered or is close to adhering, so then loosen it tomorrow or on the third day, and when you loosen the wound, replace the cloths with others and renew the ointment.
If thin pus comes out of the hidden (mukhaba') after a long time, do not despair of its adherence, for its healing is near, because among people some have slow flesh growth in their wounds and some the opposite of that.
If immature pus comes out of the hidden (mukhaba') after many days, know that the hidden (mukhaba') has not adhered. If the adherence of the hidden (mukhaba') is slow and its matter prolonged, increase the drying of the medicines, and let the medicines be moist in their consistency and dry in their potency, such as the palm ointment if an extra amount of qalqatar has been added to it.
And among the most effective things that it is treated with is that the palm ointment is taken and dissolved with rose oil and the aged wine, moderate in its consistency, is sprinkled on it, then you knead it with it and use it.
And a similar action may be done, if the palm ointment is not available to you, with honey, if you cook it until it thickens and use it, or you take myrrh, aloe and frankincense, and grind all of it and sprinkle it on the honey while it is on the fire, then spread it on a cloth and bind it on the hidden (mukhaba'), or spread on it honey that you've cooked until it thickens, and sprinkle the drugs on it, and place the cloth on it and bind it.
And long ziravand, the root of the German iris, cress flour and centaury may be used in such a case, whether you use them singly or combined, you grind them, sift them, and sprinkle them on the honey I described and use it.
If the matter of the hidden (mukhaba') is prolonged and it does not heal with this treatment, know that there is corruption in its depth and it has affected the bones or the rest of the hard nerves or ligaments, so treat it with the treatment of the fistula as described above.
Chapter eighty-nine on the treatment of felon, bruised nail, and amputation. الأصبع الزائدة وشق التحام الأصابع
The felon is a lot of flesh that grows under the nail of the thumb of the hand or foot, and perhaps it grows in the rest of the fingers. If it is prolonged and its treatment is neglected, it swells with a hot swelling, or it becomes badly corrupted until it eats the root of the nail, and perhaps it spoils it all. Perhaps the corruption reaches the bone until it has a foul odor, and the tip of the finger becomes wide, and its color becomes livid. So if you treat it with what we mentioned in the division and your treatment does not succeed, then you should cut with iron all the excess that grows from the nail, then cauterize the wound after that, for cauterization in this is very beneficial.
But if the bone is healthy, and the nail is also healthy, and the containing corner of the nail has pressed the flesh inwards and made it sting and hurt it, then you should put a thin probe under the corner of the nail that stings the flesh and lift it up and cut that flesh gently and put on what remains of the flesh of the burning corrosive medicines until it all goes away, then treat it with ointments until it heals.
But if the corruption has affected the bone, then you should cut that bone and remove it, for it will not heal as long as there is corrupt bone in it at all.
If you see the erosion and corruption running in the finger, then cut it in one of the phalanges as mentioned previously, then treat it until it heals.
If the nail is struck or bruised and there is severe pain in it, then it is necessary to phlebotomize the patient first, then incise the nail with a sharp scalpel, an oblique incision from top to bottom, and be careful not to reach the flesh under the nail with the incision, for you will cause severe pain to the patient and it will be a reason for the growth of excess flesh in the place, then treat the place with something that soothes the pains.
As for the extra finger that is generated in some people's hands, perhaps it is all fleshy, and perhaps some of it has bones, and perhaps it has a nail, and some of it grows at the base of the joint of some fingers, or it grows in some of the phalanges of the finger. The one that grows in some of the phalanges of the finger does not move, and the one that grows at the joint of the finger may move. So what is fleshy, cutting it is easy, and that is to cut it at its root with a wide scalpel. But the one that grows at the base of the joint, its treatment is difficult, so avoid cutting it.
As for the one that grows in the finger at one of the phalanges, it should first be cut flesh, a circular cut to the bone, then saw the bone with one of those saws suitable for that, then treat it until it heals.
As for the adhesion that occurs to the fingers with each other, this often happens, and this is something that a person is born with, and it happens when a wound heals, or a burn from fire, and so on. It is necessary to incise that adhesion until the fingers return to their natural form, then put between them wicks or rags soaked in rose oil so that they do not adhere quickly, and separate them, or put between them a thin sheet of lead until the place heals as it should.
Likewise, if the adhesion occurs to some of the fingers with the palm, then incise that adhesion according to what is feasible and what corrects the shape of the member.
Chapter ninety on severing varicose veins.
Varicose veins are twisted, thick veins filled with black residues that occur in most parts of the body, and most commonly in the legs, especially in porters, donkey drivers, and laborers.
First, one should use strong shaking of the body from black bile several times, then bleed the basilic vein.
As for its treatment with iron, it is of two types: one is to incise and extract the black blood, and the other is to extract the vein entirely.
As for incising it, it is done in this way: first, the leg is bathed with very hot water until the thick, turbid blood dissolves, then the patient's leg is tied from above his thigh to below his knee with a turban, then the vein is incised in one, two, or three wide incisions, then the black blood is extracted by hand from the bottom of the leg upwards and from top to bottom until the amount of blood that you see sufficient or what the patient's strength can tolerate is extracted, then it is bandaged and the patient is instructed to avoid foods that produce black bile, and the purging and bleeding are repeated whenever the veins become full and that harms the patient.
As for extracting it, it is done in this way: the patient's leg is shaved if it has a lot of hair, then he enters the bath and his leg is bathed with hot water until it reddens and the vein swells, or he exercises vigorously if he does not have a bath until the limb warms up.
Then the skin opposite the vein is incised lengthwise, either at its end near the knee or below it near the heel, then the skin is opened with hooks and the vein is skinned from all sides until it is exposed to the touch, and when it appears, you see it bright red, and when it is free from the skin, you see it also like a tendon.
Then a probe is inserted under it until it rises and emerges from the skin, then it is hung with a smooth, blunt hook.
Then another incision is made near that incision about three fingers wide, then the skin is peeled off the vein until it appears. ثمّ ارفعه بالمرود كما فعلت وعلّقه بصنّارة أخرى كما فعلت أوّلا،
Then make another incision or many incisions if you need to.
Then pull it and cut it at the end of the incision near the heel, then pull it and extract it until it comes out of the second incision, then pull it to the incision above it, and do this until you pull it from the third incision, the highest of all the incisions, until when all of it comes out, cut it.
If it does not respond to pulling and extraction, insert a needle with a strong, doubled thread and tie it and pull it, and insert the probe under it and twist it with your hand in every direction until it comes out, and be careful that it does not break, for if it breaks, it will be very difficult for you to extract it and it will cause harm to the patient.
When you have extracted all of it, put wool dipped in wine and rose oil or oil on the wounds and treat it until it heals.
If the varicose vein is convulsive, with curves and twists in different directions, and not straight as we have said, then it is necessary to incise it at each of its curves and twists, then hang it with hooks until you extract it entirely.
Be careful when incising it not to cut or injure the vein, for it will make it difficult for you to extract it, so be very careful.
Image of the hook with which the varicose vein is incised:
Image of the blind fishhook:
It should not have a curvature like other hooks. ولا تكون حادّة الطرف لئلّا تجرح العرق
And it should be thick at the bend, smooth, because if it were thin, it would cut the vein/vessel with its thinness. بل يكون لها فضل غلظ كما قلنا
Chapter ninety-one on phthisis of the civilized perspiration.
This vein is generated in the legs in hot countries like the Hijaz and the countries of the Arabs, and in hot, dry, and infertile countries.
And perhaps it is generated in other places of the body other than the legs.
And its generation is from putrefaction that occurs under the skin, as it occurs inside living bodies, worms, bottle gourds, and the worms generated between the skin and the flesh.
And the sign of the beginning of the occurrence of this vein is that severe inflammation occurs in the leg. ثمّ يتنفّط الموضع.
So when its tip appears, it should be wrapped around a small piece of lead, whose weight is from one dirham to two dirhams. وتعقده وتترك الرصاص معلّقا من الساق.
So whenever something comes out of it, wrap it in the lead and tie it. فإن طال كثيرا فاقطع بعضه ولفّ الباقى.
Therefore, it should be handled gently and pulled little by little until it comes out completely and nothing of it remains in the body.
And from this vein, in some people, may come out what is five cubits and ten in length. وقد بلغنى أنّه خرج لرجل من عشرين شبرا.
If it breaks for you while you are treating it, insert a probe into its hole. وبطّه بطّا طويلا مع البدن حتّى يتفرّغ كلّ ما فيه من مادّة.
And this vein may have many branches, especially if it appears in the joint of the leg or in the leg itself. فيحدث له أفواه كثيرة ويخرج من كلّ فم شعبة.
So treat it as we mentioned in the division and with what preceded.
Chapter ninety-two on the incision on the worms generated under the smallpox called the disease of cattle
This disease is called in some of our countries the disease of cattle because it often happens to cattle.
It is a single small worm that is generated between the skin and the flesh and creeps in the whole body, ascending and descending, becoming apparent to the sense when it creeps from one organ to another until it pierces a place in the skin and comes out.
And its formation is from the putrefaction of some of the humors, as happens to worms, snakes, and حب القرع (probably a type of skin eruption or growth) in the belly.
And what is expected of its harm is that if it creeps in the body and rises to the head and reaches the eye, it may open it and come out, thus destroying the eye, and this happens often.
So, if you want to treat it and remove it, it can only be done when it creeps and appears to the sense, so you should tie what is above it and below it with a good tight bandage, then cut over it and remove it.
If it sinks into the flesh and you do not find it, then apply cauterization with fire to the place until you burn it.
And the greatest thing that is expected is its corruption of the eye, as we have said, so if you see that it has gone to the head near the eye, then tie tightly under it on the forehead, then cut over it and take it out.
And it is necessary that the sick person take care to purify his body with medicines that purge the putrid, bad humors and be cautious of foods that generate putrefaction.
Chapter ninety-three, on incising the illness that is known as an abscess.
This disease, which is called "al-nafir" in our country, is a pain that occurs in some of the limbs, then moves from one limb to another, and I have seen it as I describe it to you.
I was called to a sick woman in one of the rural areas, and she uncovered her arm and showed me a slight swelling in the vein of the forearm, and after an hour passed I saw that swelling crawling along the ulna as a worm crawls, ascending to her shoulder as quickly as could be, like mercury when it flows from place to place.
So the pain went away from that place and settled in the shoulder.
Then I sat for an hour, and it ran throughout the body until it went into the other arm.
Then she told me that it goes around her whole body as I saw, so I marveled at the speed of its movement from limb to limb, and I had not previously seen this disease with my own eyes in this manner except that I saw a group of people who experience the pain moving from limb to limb, but I hadn't seen it with my own eyes like I saw it in this woman. I did not estimate that except that it must be because the woman was from the Bedouins, having a dry body and exposed veins, so from this, that moving wind became apparent to the senses, and it necessarily does not appear in this manner in those of comfort with moist bodies and hidden veins.
So if you want to treat it and its possessor feels the pain, if it appears to you visibly as we mentioned, then tie tightly above it and below it with haste and incise upon it until that trapped wind comes out and cauterize the place.
But if you do not see it with your eyes, then treat it by shaking the body and with that which expels the winds and disperses them, such as asafoetida seeds and sagapenum seeds and similar medications.
Chapter ninety-four on removing arrows.
Arrows may differ according to their types and according to the places in which they are located in the body.
As for their differences according to their types, there are large ones and small ones, and there are hollow glass ones and solid glass ones, and some have three corners and four corners, and some have tongues and some have splinters.
As for those that are according to the organs in which they are located, they are of two types: either the organs are from the main hollow organs such as the brain, heart, liver, lung, kidneys, intestines, bladder, and the like. فمتى وقع سهم فى أحد هذه الأعضاء وظهرت لك علامات الموت التى أنا واصفها لك بعد فينبغى أن تجتنب إخراج ذلك السهم منها فإنّ الموت يلحق صاحبها فى أكثر الأحوال،
And of the signs of the brain, if an arrow hits it and penetrates the bone and wounds the peritoneum which is on the brain, then there results from that a severe headache, nausea, dizziness, redness in the eyes, redness of the tongue, convulsions, confusion of mind, vomiting, and sometimes blood comes out of the nostrils or ears, and sometimes speech is cut off and the voice is gone, and a white moisture resembling porridge comes out of the place of the wound and a liquid like the water of the flesh flows from it.
If these signs appear to you, then refrain from treating the patient and removing the arrow if it has not come out.
As for the signs of the arrow if it hits the heart and is close to the left breast and feels as if it has been driven into something solid and not into something empty, and perhaps the arrow has a movement resembling the movement of the pulse, and black blood flows from the wound, and this is followed by coldness of the extremities, cold sweat, and fainting, then know that death is certainly coming.
And a sign of the arrow if it wounds the lung is the exit of frothy blood from the wound, and the vessels which are next to the neck swell, and the color of the patient changes, and he breathes heavily and asks to inhale cold air.
If the arrow hits the diaphragm which is in the chest, then it is close to the small ribs, and the breathing is great with severe pain and sighing, and all the members of the shoulders move.
And if the arrow hits the liver, severe pain follows, and blood comes out of the wound which resembles the liver in its redness.
And if the arrow hits the stomach, then perhaps something undigested from the food comes out of the wound, and the matter is clear.
And if the arrow hits the abdomen and is stuck in it and something from the stool, or the fat, or an intestine which has been perforated comes out of the wound, then there is no hope in treating it or in removing the arrow.
If the arrow is in the bladder and the urine comes out and something protrudes from it to the outside and the pain intensifies on the patient, then know that he is doomed.
As for the other organs, like the face, eye, throat, shoulder, arm, vertebrae of the back, collarbone, thigh, leg, and the like of the organs, they are usually saved when the arrow does not encounter an artery or nerve and the arrow is not poisoned.
And I tell you some of what I have witnessed concerning these arrows so that you may deduce from that your treatment.
That is, an arrow had hit a man in the corner of his eye at the root of the nose, so I removed it for him from the other side under the lobe of the ear, and he healed and no harm occurred in his eye.
And I removed another arrow for a Jew that had struck him in the lobe of his eye under the lower eyelid, and the arrow had hidden, and I only reached the small end that adheres to the wood, and it was a large arrow from the arrows of the compound bow, square iron, smooth, and there were no barbs on it, so the Jew healed and no bad incident occurred in his eye.
And I removed another arrow from the throat of a Christian, and the arrow was an Arabian one, which is the one that has two barbs, so I made an incision on him between the jugular veins, and it had sunk into his throat, so I gently handled it until I removed it, and the Christian was saved and healed.
And I removed another arrow from a man who had been hit in his abdomen, and we estimated that he would die from it, but when he remained for thirty days or so and nothing changed in his condition, I made an incision on the arrow and maneuvered it and removed it, and he healed and no bad incident occurred.
And I saw a man who had been hit by an arrow in his back, and the wound healed over him, but when it was after seven years the arrow came out at the root of his thigh.
And I saw a woman who had been hit by an arrow in her abdomen, and the wound healed and the arrow remained, and nothing changed in her condition, nor did she feel any harm from it in any of her natural actions.
And I saw another man who had been hit by an arrow in his face, and the wound healed and he felt little pain from it.
And many are like this.
And I tell you how to remove some arrows so that you make that a measure and a guide to what I have not mentioned, because the parts of this craft and its details cannot be grasped by description nor can a book encompass them, but rather the skillful craftsman measures by the little on the much and by what is present on what is absent, and he infers a new work and a new instrument when strange incidents descend from this craft.
So I say that arrows are removed from the organs in which they are stuck in two ways: either by pulling from the place from which it entered, or from the opposite direction.
And those which come out from where they entered, either the arrow is protruding in a fleshy place, so it is pulled and removed. If it does not respond to coming out at the time it fell, then it is necessary for you to leave it for days until the flesh around it rots so that it is easy to pull and remove it.
Similarly, if it is stuck in a bone and does not respond to coming out, then also leave it for days and return to pulling and moving it every day, then it will come out.
If it does not respond to coming out after days, then it is necessary to pierce around the arrow in the bone itself from every side with a fine drill until you widen it for the arrow, then pull it and remove it.
As for if the arrow has hidden in a place in the body and is hidden from feeling, then examine it with a probe. If you feel it, then pull it with some instruments that are suitable for pulling it. If you cannot do it because of the narrowness of the wound and the distance of the arrow in the depth and there is no bone, nerve, or vein there, then make an incision on it until the wound widens and you are able to reach the arrow until you remove it.
If it has two barbs, grasp them, then free the flesh stuck in them from every side with every trick you can do that, and endeavor, if you cannot free the flesh, to break the barbs and twist them until you are free.
And when you try to remove the arrow in whatever place it may be, then use the twisting of your hand with the forceps to all sides until you free it, and be extremely gentle lest the arrow break and it be difficult for you to pull and remove it.
If you cannot do it at your time, then leave it for days until those fleshes around it rot, then return to it, for it will be easy then. If bleeding obstructs you, then use what we mentioned of treatment in its chapter.
And guard yourself from cutting a vein, nerve, or tendon, and use a trick in every way you can free the arrow, and let that be gently, slowly, and steadily as I described to you.
And it is necessary for you, when you pull the arrow, to put the patient in the shape he was in when the arrow struck, for that is more suitable. If you cannot do that, then use whatever shapes you can.
As for the arrow which comes out from the opposite direction, either something of it has protruded to the outside, or you find the end of the arrow by feeling from above the skin near and see it protruding, so make an incision on it, and let the incision be to the extent that the forceps can fit in it, then pull it, for it is easy to come out.
If it sticks in a bone, then twist your hand in a circle until the arrow affects the bone and widens for itself, then pull it. Otherwise, leave it for days, then return to it until it comes out.
If the shaft of the arrow is in it, then push it with it. If the shaft has fallen and you want to use pushing, then insert the hollow instrument so that its cavity enters the tail of the arrow, then push it with it.
If the arrow is hollow, then push it with an instrument that enters that cavity, for the arrow is easily removed by that.
If the arrow is poisoned, then it is necessary to cut away all the flesh that has become poisoned in it, if you can do that, then treat it with what is suitable for that.
If the arrow is in the chest, or in the abdomen, or in the bladder, or in the side, and it is close to what can be felt with the probe and you can make an incision on it, then make an incision and guard yourself from cutting a vein or nerve and remove it, then stitch the wound if it needs stitching, then treat it until it heals.
The image of the forceps with which arrows are pulled:
Its ends are like the beak of a bird, made as if it were a file; if it grips an arrow or something, it will not let it go.
And various types of it are made, large, small, and medium, all according to the size of the arrow, its smallness, the width of the wound, and its narrowness.
Image of the hollow cannon:
Image of the solid midfagh:
This is solid at the end, like the probe, to facilitate its entry into the hollow arrow and pushing it.
Chapter Ninety-Five on Phlebotomy
The veins that are customarily phlebotomized in the body are thirty veins, of which sixteen are in the head. العرقان النابضان اللذان خلف الأذنين المعروفان بالحسيسين والشريانان اللذان فى الصدغين الظاهران والعرقان اللذان فى مأقى العينين المعروفان بالناظرين والعرق المنتصب فى وسط الجبهة والعرق الذى فى طرف الأنف والودجان اللذان فى العنق والعرقان اللذان فى الشفة العليا من الفم والعرقان اللذان فى الشفة السفلى وهذه العروق المعروفة بالجهارك والعرقان اللذان تحت اللسان
