As for the veins that are phlebotomized in the arm and hand, they are five veins: one of them is the Cephalic, and it is from the lateral side, and the common people call it the vein of the head; and the Akhal, and it is the middle vein, and it is composed of a branch of the Basilic and a branch of the Cephalic, and the common people call it the vein of the body; and the Basilic, and it is located on the medial side, and it is also called the Axillary, and the common people call it the vein of the abdomen; and the ligament of the arm, which is located on the ulna, and it is the one that adheres to it, and it is the one that appears clearly above the thumb; and the Aslim, and it is the vein that is between the little finger and the ring finger, it has two branches.
And in the leg and foot are three veins: one of them is the one under the hollow of the knee from the lateral side; and the second is the Saphenous, and its place is at the ankle from the medial side; and the Sciatic vein, and its place is at the heel from the lateral side.
And in the other leg are three veins like it.
As for the two veins that are behind the ears, the benefit of their phlebotomy is for chronic colds, migraine, ringworm, and chronic malignant head ulcers.
And the way to phlebotomize them is as I describe: that you shave the head of the sick person and rub its back in the place of the two veins with a rough cloth, rubbing it well, then the sick person strangles his neck with a turban until the two veins appear, and their place is behind the ears in the two low places of the head, so you search for them with your finger, and where you feel their pulse under your finger, there mark with ink, then you take a scalpel, which is known as the Nashal, then you insert it under the vein in the skin until the scalpel reaches the bone, then you raise your hand with the vein and skin upwards and cut the vein with the skin, a severed cut, and the length of the cut is the extent of two closed fingers or so, and you let out the amount of blood that you want, then you tie them with bandages and leave them until they heal.
And their cutting and cauterization have been mentioned at the beginning of the book.
As for the two arteries that are in the temples, the benefit of their phlebotomy is for chronic migraine, severe headache, constant ophthalmia, and the flow of acute humors pouring into the eyes.
And the way to phlebotomize them is as I describe to you: the sick person tightens his neck with a turban until the two veins appear clearly to the touch, and their pulse becomes clear under your finger, then you mark with ink, then you lift the skin from above the vein up with your index finger, and you insert the Nashal scalpel from below, and you lift the vein upwards and sever it as you did with the other two veins, and you let out the blood according to your need, then you loosen the sick person's strangulation and place your finger on the vein for an hour, then you put cotton and a bandage on it and tie it tightly from above and leave it until it heals.
And their mention, cutting, and scraping have been mentioned at the beginning of the book.
As for the phlebotomy of the forehead vein, its benefit after the phlebotomy of the Cephalic is for chronic facial ailments such as ringworm, ulcers, and unsightly redness.
And the way to phlebotomize it is as I describe to you: the sick person strangles his neck with a turban until the vein appears, then you take the instrument called the Axe, and this is its picture:
You place the projecting fork that is at the head of the axe on the vein itself and strike from above with a comb or something similar in its manner, and you let the blood flow to the extent that you desire, then you loosen the tourniquet of the sick person and tighten it until he recovers. And it may be opened with a wide lancet, except that it is not appropriate for the lancet to be sharp-tipped like other lancets, but rather be slightly wide-tipped, and you bleed it obliquely because the bone is near, so perhaps the lancet might break in it if it is thin.
As for the two veins that are in the corners of the eyes, their benefit is for eye ailments like scabies, redness, eyelashes, and diseases of the face.
And as for the way of bleeding them, it is that the sick person tightens his neck with a turban, then you bleed them while you are standing at his head, and let the bleeding be slightly obliquely lengthwise with a small, slightly wide lancet, for the place has no flesh, so if the lancet is thin-tipped, perhaps it might break.
Then you let out the blood you need and put cotton on them and tighten them for one night, then you loosen them.
And as for bleeding the vein of the nose, it is beneficial for acute fever and severe headache and for diseases of the face like the red erysipelas that occurs in the nose, especially if it is chronic.
And the way to bleed it is that the sick person tightens his neck, then you hold his nose with your left hand and take a thin, long lancet and insert it in the middle of the tip of the nose itself between the partitions of the nose straight, because the vein does not appear to the senses there, so the blood will come out immediately.
And it is appropriate that you deepen your hand with the lancet a little and let out the blood you need, then bandage it for a night, for it will heal quickly.
As for the two external jugular veins, the benefit of bleeding them is for shortness of breath, the beginning of leprosy, and melancholic diseases that occur on the surface of the skin, such as black vitiligo, scabies, malignant ulcers, and eating sores.
And the way of bleeding them is that the ill person tightens below them on his neck with a bandage, and the practitioner stands at the head of the sick person, and the sick person is sitting on a chair, then you bleed the vein lengthwise with a slightly wide bleeding, then you let out a moderate amount of blood, or according to what you see of the need for that, then you do likewise with the other vein, then you loosen the bandage and tighten the two veins with a moderate tightening, lest the sick person suffocate, and you leave it until the next day, for the wound will heal.
As for the veins of the jaw, the benefit of bleeding them after bleeding the cephalic vein is that it benefits from thrush in the mouth, corruption of the gums, malignant ulcers, chapped lips, and malignant ulcers that are in the nose and around it.
And the way of bleeding them is that you sit the sick person in front of you and tighten his neck with a turban, then you turn his lips and look at the two veins that you see, one on the right of the lip and the other on its left, and you distinguish them by their blackness, and that is also because around them are thin black veins, so you cut them with a severed cut.
If it is difficult for you and you do not know which they are, then aim to cut the largest and clearest of them, and likewise do with the two veins that are in the lower lip.
As for the two veins that are under the tongue, the benefit of bleeding them after bleeding the cephalic vein is for the ulcers that are in the throat from disease of the uvula and diseases of the mouth.
And the way of bleeding them is that you sit the sick person between your hands facing the sun and lift his tongue and look under the tongue on one side for a vein and on the other side for a vein, and their color is blackish, so you bleed them and be careful not to go deep in cutting them, for beneath them are arteries, so perhaps bleeding might occur from those arteries.
As for the three veins that are bled in the elbow, they are the ones that are customarily bled in all people, and their bleeding is in two ways: either by piercing with a wide basil-shaped lancet or an olive-shaped one tending towards thinness, or by incising with a knife-shaped lancet, which is the scalpel [and this is its image].
Image of the wide basil-shaped lancet:
It should be wide, as you see, so that it is suitable for opening the hollow, full, prominent, visible, thick veins that contain thick, turbid blood.
And this is a picture of the olive-shaped lancet:
And this lancet is less wide and thinner at the tip, suitable for phlebotomizing thin veins that contain thin, choleric blood. وهذه صورة المبضع النشل:
This is the lancet that is suitable for incision, and there are wide and thin types of it, depending on the width and narrowness of the veins, and this may be inferred from others, and it is well known among artisans.
As for the basilic vein, which is one of these three veins, the benefit of its phlebotomy is that it draws blood from the ailments that are under the throat and neck, from what is next to the chest and abdomen.
And the phlebotomist should be cautious when performing phlebotomy on it and be careful of it, for beneath it is an artery, and if he makes a mistake and increases the insertion of the lancet, he will cut that artery, causing bleeding, so therefore, its phlebotomy should not be with a piercing lancet, but rather by incision with the lancet.
If the basilic vein does not appear clearly, then it should be avoided and another should be used, or some of its branches should be sought, or the arm's tendon should be phlebotomized in its place, for it is clear, and incise it with the lancet as we have said.
If you want to phlebotomize it specifically, then it is necessary before tightening the arm to feel the place until you identify the location of the pulse, then mark it with ink, then tie the arm and incise the vein with an oblique incision with the lancet as we said, and make sure that the strike falls away from the location of the artery.
And when you see, when tightening the bandage, swelling in the place that you marked with ink, then that swelling is the swelling of the artery, so avoid it.
If you see the blood, at the time of phlebotomy, jumping as the urine of a child jumps, and the blood is thin and red, then know that it is from the blood of the artery, so then immediately put your finger on it for a long time, then remove your finger, and if the blood stops, as it often does, then tie the arm and leave it.
And warn the patient against neglecting it, and let it be on his neck, and let him not move it for days until he recovers.
If the blood does not stop and overcomes you, and you do not have medicine at your disposal, then sever the artery if it appears to you, for its ends will contract and the blood will stop.
Or take a pistachio shell and split it, and take one half and tighten it on the location of the vein tightly with a bandage and padding until the next day.
Then if the blood stops, and if not, then treat it with what was mentioned previously regarding placing the styptic powders.
And stopping its blood is not difficult in most cases because of the smallness of the wound and the effectiveness of the bandage on the arm, so know this.
As for the Akhal vein, the benefit of its phlebotomy is that it draws blood from the top of the head and the lower part of the body because it is composed of a branch from the Basilic vein and a branch from the Cephalic vein, as we have said.
And the phlebotomist should be cautious when performing phlebotomy on it, because there is a nerve underneath it, and if he goes too deep with the lancet and hits the nerve, numbness will occur in it, which is difficult to heal, and it may not heal at all.
This nerve is often perceptible to the touch. If it is hidden in some people and is thin and not apparent, then you should make your incision with the lancet lengthwise and avoid the nerve as much as possible. If the vein is between two nerves, then incise the vein lengthwise.
As for the Cephalic vein, the benefit of its phlebotomy is that it draws blood from the head and is useful for eye diseases. It is recommended for this vein, especially, if you want to perform phlebotomy on it, to do it by puncturing with the olive-shaped lancet or the broad basil-shaped lancet because it is the safest of all veins, as there is no artery or nerve underneath it, except that you should avoid the head of the muscle with the lancet and look for the soft spot.
It does not harm it if it is not hit on the first try to repeat the phlebotomy several times, except that it may swell in some people if it is not bled on the first try, but that swelling does not harm it at all.
As for the method of phlebotomy, its complications, and what should precede it in its preparation, the first thing you should know is that phlebotomy is either used to maintain health only or used in diseases. The one used to maintain and sustain health and to prevent the occurrence of diseases should be performed on one of the three veins in the elbow, namely the Cephalic, the Akhal, and the Basilic, and the phlebotomy should be performed at the beginning of spring when signs of fullness appear, and it should be done on Sunday or Tuesday after three hours of daylight have passed.
As for the phlebotomy used in diseases, it has no specific time, but whenever the need and necessity for it arises, whether at night or day, at any hour and at any time.
Children should not be bled until they reach fourteen years of age, and the elderly who have passed sixty years of age should not be bled.
If someone intends to have phlebotomy for any reason, it is necessary to cleanse his intestines before phlebotomy with a mild enema if there is a lot of retained waste, so that excess putrid matter that harms the main organs is not drawn from the intestines to the veins during phlebotomy.
Do not bleed the bloated, the drunk, or the intoxicated until that condition has passed from them. Also, avoid phlebotomy immediately after fever, vomiting, diarrhea, excessive intercourse, fatigue, exercise, staying up late, fasting, and anything that weakens the strength, whether physical or mental.
Then consider thinning the humors before that if the blood is thick, with foods, drinks, and medicines, if possible.
Then he should enter the bath if there is no impediment preventing him, or he should engage in some exercise so that the blood thins. And make his phlebotomy in the middle of the day, as we have said. He should aim to clear his mind that day of all bad psychological symptoms such as worries, anger, and fear, and of all physical symptoms such as excessive fatigue and exhaustion, intercourse, and so on.
And have present in his company things that people are accustomed to using, such as various perfumes, fragrant herbs, entertainment, and so on, each person according to what they can afford.
Then the phlebotomist sits on a cushion that is higher than the cushion on which the person being bled sits.
Then he extends his arm, and the phlebotomist rubs it with his hand two or three times, then tightens the bandage with the cord and twists it two or three times. The tightening should be moderate because if the tightening is not moderate, either by excessive tightening, it will prevent the flow of blood, or if it is too loose, it will also prevent the flow of blood.
Then, after tightening, the person being bled should rub his hands together until the veins swell and become apparent to the touch. Then the phlebotomist wipes the lancet with a little old oil, specifically. Then he places his index finger of his left hand on the vein itself, slightly below the spot where he intends to bleed, so that the vein does not shrink and avoid the strike, because some veins are like tendons, shrinking during phlebotomy, and some are filled with air. So when you place the lancet on them, they sink under the lancet and deceive the phlebotomist, and the lancet does not open the vein. And if it does open it, the opening will be narrow.
Therefore, the phlebotomist should be firm and take his time in all these matters.
Then he lowers the lancet. If it opens the vein on that attempt, then fine, otherwise, he should try again a little below that spot or above it quickly if the area has not swollen.
If it swells or the patient becomes distressed, leave it for a day or two, and do not tighten the bandage, as it may cause a hot swelling. And he should not enter the bath.
Then he can resume phlebotomy if he wishes.
If the lancet punctures and the opening is small and the blood flow is thin, and you fear that the desired amount of blood will not come out, then return the lancet to the same hole gently and straight, and increase the opening slightly. Do this quickly before the area swells, because in many people the area may swell at the small opening. If you see that it has swollen, do not try again at all, as it will not help you. Put some oil cake on it, as it facilitates the flow of blood, and it is better in this place than the oil itself and all other ointments. Likewise, use oil cake in all your vein phlebotomy when the blood flow is difficult. Theriac Faruq and Shakhizaniya can also do this if either of them is placed on the area, as the blood thins and dissolves if it is thick.
If a large swelling occurs at the site of phlebotomy, which often happens, especially for those who have only been bled that one time or if the opening of the vein was small, then quickly place a sponge dipped in slightly warmed water and salt on the swelling and tighten it for an hour, and it will dissolve.
You should do this after the blood has completely come out from the vein itself or from another vein.
If something of blackness or greenness remains in the area after a few days, it does not harm. فإن أحببت فاحمل عليه شيئا من الصبر والمرّ المحلولين او شيئا من عصارة الفودنج ونحوه
Swelling and a bump often occur during the phlebotomy of the basilic vein, so place your hand on it, and if you find it softens when you press it, then it is a bad bump, so be careful not to put anything on it that we have mentioned because it might bleed arterial blood. But bandage it with something astringent to make the area firm, then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
It is necessary to extract blood from every person according to his strength and what appears of the dominant color of the blood. For, if the blood is black, let it out until it turns red, and similarly, if you see it thick, let it out until it thins, and likewise, if it is sharp until its sharpness is gone.
It is necessary for whoever is full-bodied and strong and needs to remove blood all at once to widen the phlebotomy of the vein and let the lancet be wide. And whoever is weak, then the opposite of that. It is best to extract it in rounds and to have the hole narrow.
The best way to perform phlebotomy is obliquely, angled and with an incision, not a stab.
This type of phlebotomy is safe from bleeding and from cutting a nerve, and it is better and safer than horizontal or vertical phlebotomy.
Whoever usually faints during phlebotomy, it is necessary to feed him before the phlebotomy some bread soaked in sour pomegranate juice or oxymel if he is hot-tempered, and let his blood out in three or four rounds. If he has a cold temperament, he should take before the phlebotomy bread soaked in a drink of water-mint or in honey drink sweetened with spices or in basil-flavored drink.
If fainting occurs during phlebotomy, and its cause is the loss of a large amount of blood, he should be given meat broth and a thin basil drink. He should use rose water perfume and have his chest gargled with it, and all other things we have mentioned in the section on fainting caused by bloodletting.
As for whoever wants to relax his arm and let his blood flow again, it is necessary for someone whose phlebotomy was for excessive bloodletting and whose strength is weak, to release blood little by little, according to his strength, on consecutive days.
As for whoever wants to relax his arm and let his blood flow again, and whose body is strong, let him do so seven or nine hours after his first phlebotomy.
As for whoever wants to draw blood from his body to the opposite side to which it has flowed, he should be treated on the second or third day.
As for whoever has a lot of blood in his body that has become hot and acute and has caused fever, it is necessary to remove the blood from him all at once and take out a large amount of it and widen the opening until fainting occurs, after having examined all the conditions of phlebotomy, and to place your hand on his pulse when the blood is flowing so that death does not occur instead of fainting. This happens often if the phlebotomist is ignorant and negligent.
It is not necessary, if you want to loosen the arm and release the blood again, and the mouth of the vein has closed and the blood flow is difficult, to press on it hard or twist with force because that is very bad. Rather, either leave it until you perform phlebotomy again, or remove with the blade of the lancet what has clotted from the blood at the mouth of the vein, or apply to it some salt that has been dissolved in water, or apply some theriac or scammony, and press gently until the blood comes out.
If the vein is swollen, leave it alone and do not touch it until the swelling subsides.
If there is a need to release blood again, it is unavoidable; then either bleed him above that area, or bleed him in the other arm or in the other vein.
As for phlebotomy of the median cubital vein, it is phlebotomized as a substitute for the median and the basilic veins if they are not found or are hidden, for it is composed of the two.
The method of its phlebotomy is that the patient puts his hand in hot water until the forearm becomes red and the vein appears clearly, then you tie it above it with the bandage slightly, moderately tight, then you phlebotomize the vein obliquely, a little, not horizontally or vertically, and let the opening be wide and let your phlebotomy be a little above the wrist joint.
If blood flow is difficult, put the hand back into the container with hot water and let the blood flow into the water until your need is met. If you are in the summer days, you might not need to put the hand back into the water. Mostly, flowing of the blood into hot water is practiced during winter.
Phlebotomy of this vein is safer than all veins, because there is no pulsating vein or nerve beneath it.
As for phlebotomy of the cephalic vein of the right hand, it is beneficial for liver ailments, and the method of its phlebotomy is to tie the wrist with a bandage or with your hand after putting it in hot water until the vein swells and becomes very clear to the touch, then you phlebotomize it slightly obliquely, and even if you cut it completely, it will not harm it at all. Be careful not to go deep with your lancet because the nerve of the fingers is beneath it and the area is devoid of flesh.
Then put the hand back into the hot water and let the blood flow in it. If you do not return it to the hot water, the blood will clot at the mouth of the vein and stop flowing. Once you have removed the required amount of blood, put some fat and salt on the vein so that it does not heal quickly. The same applies to every narrow branch.
As for the benefit of phlebotomizing it from the left hand, it is beneficial for spleen ailments, and similarly you do in its phlebotomy as you did with the second one, the same way.
As for the phlebotomy of the saphenous vein, its benefit is for diseases in the lower body such as uterine ailments, menstrual retention, kidney diseases, chronic ulcers of the thighs and legs, and similar diseases.
The method of its phlebotomy is to put his leg in hot water and massage it until the veins flow, then tie it above the ankle joint. The vein is located at the heel, visible towards the big toe, and it branches out into many branches on the leg. So, phlebotomize it at its widest branch, or at the heel at its confluence. This is best and safest.
If you phlebotomize it on the surface of the leg, be careful of the nerves underneath on the surface of the leg, and make your phlebotomy oblique, as if you intend to cut it, and the lancet should be sharp.
If the blood flow is difficult, let him put his leg back in the hot water and leave the blood to flow until it drains.
If the phlebotomist misses the vein with the phlebotomy in the first round, let him repeat the phlebotomy slightly above it, for the area is safe and there is no fear of a problem if you are careful of the nerve as we said. And similarly you do with the saphenous vein of the other leg in the same way.
As for the sciatic vein, its place as we said is at the heel of the lateral side, and the benefit of its phlebotomy is for hip pain if it is due to hot blood. The method of its phlebotomy is to have the patient enter the bath quickly and tie his leg from the hip to four fingers above the heel with a thin, long bandage, because it does not appear otherwise.
When it appears, phlebotomize it in any case you can, either obliquely, which is better, or sever it completely or make an incision, because its place is safe.
It is very hidden in most people. If you do not find it and it does not appear to the touch at all, phlebotomize some of its branches, which are those that appear on the back of the foot towards the little and ring fingers. Beware of the nerves, and let out the amount of blood you want, then loosen the bandage and put cotton on the place of phlebotomy and bandage the area, for it heals quickly.
Chapter ninety-six on cupping and how to use it.
Its mouth opening should be two open fingers, as we have illustrated, and its depth should be half a span. ويكون فى جنبها فى نحو النصف منها ثقبة صغيرة على قدر ما تدخله الإبرة تصنع من النحاس الصينىّ او النحاس الأصفر غليظة الحاشية ملساء مستوية مجلوّة لئلّا تؤذى العضو عند وضعها عليه.
And in its center, there should be a transverse rod of copper or iron where the candle is placed with fire. This cupping glass can be made larger than what we described, and smaller, depending on the illnesses and age of the user, for the cupping glasses of children and thin people are different from the cupping glasses of men and bulky bodies.
As for how to place this cupping glass with fire on the organ, it is that you light a wick with fire from tightly woven linen or a small candle of pitch and place it on the center of the hardened rod that is in the center of the cupping glass so that the ascent of the fire upwards is towards the bottom of the cupping glass so that the body of the sick person does not burn. Then it is placed on the organ with a finger on the hole that we mentioned so that if we hold the cupping glass, what we need, the finger is removed and the vapor comes out of that hole and the cupping glass is released on the spot.
Then light the wick in the manner described and repeat it if needed.
As for the cupping glass that is used for the illness of shuwsa (a swelling/inflammation) with water, it does not have a hardened rod nor a hole, but rather it is used by filling it with water and placing it on the organ only.
And this is its image:
And this cupping glass, the larger it is to hold much water, the better it is,
Chapter ninety-seven on the application of leeches.
Leeches are mostly used on organs where cupping glasses cannot be applied, either because they are too small, like the lip and gums, etc., or because the organ is devoid of flesh, like the finger and nose, etc.
The method of using them is to select leeches that are in fresh, pure water, free from putrefaction, then leave them for a day and a night in fresh water until they become hungry and nothing remains in their bellies, then the body is first evacuated by phlebotomy or cupping, then the affected organ is rubbed until it becomes red, then the leeches are placed on it, and when they are full, they fall off.
And if it is possible to suck the place with a cupping glass, it is more effective in benefit, otherwise wash the area with vinegar, then with plenty of water, and massage and squeeze it. If the bleeding continues after the leeches fall off and it is oozing, then soak a linen cloth in cold water and place it on top until the oozing stops.
If the bleeding is profuse, sprinkle powdered alum or gallnut or similar astringents on it until the bleeding stops, or place peeled halves of green beans on the area and leave them until the beans stick to the area, then the bleeding will stop.
And it is advisable, if there is a need to reapply leeches, not to use the same leeches if others are available. If the leeches refuse to attach, then wipe the area with fresh blood, or prick the area with a needle until some blood comes out, then place them; for if they sense some blood, they will stick to the spot. If you want them to fall off, sprinkle on them some aloe, salt, or ash, and they will fall off immediately.
The third chapter on bone-setting. هذا الباب أيضا من وكيد ما يحتاج عليه فى صناعة الطبّ وهو جبر الكسر والفكّ الحادثين فى العظام.
Know, my sons, that this chapter is claimed by the ignorant among physicians and the common people, and by those who have never perused a book of the ancients on it, nor read a letter of it.
And for this reason, this art of science has become extinct in our country. وإنّى لم ألق فيه قطّ محسنا البتّة
Then I adhered to experience and practice throughout my life.
And I have depicted for you in this chapter all that my knowledge encompassed and my experience has gone through, after I brought it closer to you and purified it from the branches of prolixity, and I summarized it to the utmost brevity and clarified it to the utmost clarity.
And I have depicted for you in it many images of the instruments that are used in it, as it is an increase in clarity, as I did in the two preceding chapters.
And there is no power except in Allah, the Most High, the Great.
The first chapter, in it are sentences and summaries on the matter of bone fractures, which must be presented.
And before we begin to mention the broken and dislocated limbs one by one, it is necessary to mention at the beginning of this chapter some words and sections that first compel you and those other than you who are eager to learn this noble craft to understand them and comprehend their reality, so I say that whenever someone experiences a fracture, dislocation, contusion, or fall, it is necessary to first hasten to phlebotomize him, give him a laxative, or both, unless there is something that prevents this, such as weakness of strength, or if the one who experienced this is a boy, a senile old man, or if the weather is extremely hot or extremely cold. Then his diet should be restricted to cold vegetables, poultry meat, and kid meat, and he should be prevented from drinking alcohol, thick meats, filling up on food, and any food that fills the veins with blood, until the swelling subsides and there is no longer expectation of matter flowing to the site. At that time, let the patient return to his first regimen, which is his usual habit.
When the broken bone begins to heal, the patient should be nourished with foods that are very nourishing, thick, and firm, and contain stickiness, such as harees, rice, heads, trotters, cow tripe, eggs, soft fish, and thick drinks, and so on, for with this regimen the healing of the fracture will be faster and better.
And know that broken bones, if they are in strong men and old men, can never connect and heal to their original nature, due to the dryness and hardness of their bones, and bones that are extremely soft, like the bones of young children, may connect and heal. However, nature grows on the broken bone from all its sides something like thick glue that adheres to it and strengthens it until it sticks together and binds together until it becomes extremely strong and firm as it was before, so that nothing hinders its actions.
For this reason, it is necessary to make the patient's diet consist of foods that contain firmness, stickiness, and thickness, as we have said.
And know that the types of fractures may vary according to the difference in limbs, because a fracture of the leg bone is different from a fracture of the skull, and a fracture of the sternum is different from a fracture of the back, and so on for all the other limbs, each different from the other.
And we will mention each type of fracture explained in its chapter, detailed separately from others.
And the type of bone fracture may also vary in itself, because it may be a fragmented fracture without splinters, or it may be a fracture along the length of the bone with splinters and protrusions, either loose or not loose, and the fracture may be accompanied by a wound and a tear in the skin, or the fracture may be a minor crack.
And each type has a specific technique for its treatment, as will be mentioned in detail in its place.
And among the things by which a bone fracture is recognized are its curvature, its protuberance, its appearance to the senses, and its crepitation when you press it with your hand. So when there is no apparent curvature in the site, no crepitation, no feeling of disturbance when you palpate the bone, and the patient does not feel much pain, then there is no fracture there, but it may be a contusion, a minor fracture, or a minor crack. So it is not necessary to move it by extension and pressing at all, but apply to it the medicines that will be mentioned later that suit the site, then tighten it gently.
And know that if the bone is broken into two without splinters, but each part has moved away from its counterpart, you should immediately proceed to straighten and align it before hot swelling occurs.
If hot swelling occurs, leave it for a few days until the hot swelling subsides, then align it in any way you can with gentleness and skill.
And know that its treatment and alignment are easier than a bone that has splinters.
Then tighten it as will be mentioned.
If the bone has splinters, it is necessary to extend the broken limb from both sides, whether it is a hand or a leg, either with your hands if the limb is small, or with two ropes, or with a rope and a hand.
And let your placement of the limb be on a level surface, in its natural shape, so that when the parts of the broken bone are extended, then quickly return those protrusions to their places in every way you can with skill and gentleness, and strive your best not to cause the patient any pain or suffering with your actions, and strive your best to join one bone to its counterpart in the best way.
And it is necessary at that time to touch and palpate them with your hand. If you see something wrong there, correct and align it as much as you can.
And beware of excessive extension and strong pressing, as many ignorant people do, and they often cause hot swelling or chronic pain in the limb with their actions, as I have witnessed their actions many times.
Then, after alignment, perfection, and tightening, keep that limb still and at rest, and warn the patient not to move it during his waking and sleeping hours, and when he turns and moves, and when he defecates, and in all his movements to the best of his ability, and let him ensure that the position of the limb is a position in which he is safe from pain.
This is because whenever he feels pain or suffering when positioning the limb, he should move it to another position in which he does not feel pain, and he should ensure that this position is level and straight so that no curvature occurs in the limb when it heals.
As for how to tighten the broken limb, it is as I am describing to you.
Know that broken limbs differ in their smallness, largeness, and structure. So what is small among them, such as the arm, finger, ulna, and so on, the cloth bandages should be soft and thin, and what is thick among them, such as the thigh, back, and chest, the bandages should be wide and firm, because the wide bandage adheres to the large limb and tightens it from every side evenly, without any gaps.
Then, after you have finished aligning, apply the appropriate ointment on a soft cloth to the fracture site, then start wrapping the bandage around the fracture site itself three or four times, depending on what the limb requires, and tighten your hand slightly with the bandage, then go with it to the upper side of the fracture site with less tightening than your tightening of the fractured site, then move the wrapping slightly away from the fracture site and loosen the tightening gradually until you take something suitable from the healthy area. Then take another bandage and also wrap it around the fractured site a few times, then go with the wrapping to the lower side of the fracture, and let your action in tightening and loosening the wrapping be as we mentioned in the first upper wrapping. Then place between the bandages of the soft cloth or rags whatever levels the curvature of the fracture if there is curvature, otherwise, do not put anything in it.
Then wrap another bandage around it, then immediately apply the firm splints over these wraps if there is no swelling or hot inflammation in the limb.
If there is swelling or hot inflammation in it, apply to it what soothes that swelling and removes the swelling, and leave it for a few days, then tighten the splints on it at that time. And let the splints be made of wide, carved, and wisely prepared reed halves, or let the splints be made of sieve wood, which is made from pine, palm leaf stalks, heather, or alkali, and so on, of what is available.
And let the width of each splint be exactly like this image and this shape, except that the splint that is placed on the fracture itself should be slightly thicker and wider than the other splints.
As for the length of the splint, it is made according to what is appropriate for the broken limb, in terms of its size.
Image of the splint:
Then you tighten over the splints with another bandage, according to your first tightening exactly, then you tie from above with tight threads according to what we mentioned about the tightening, which is that your tightening on the site of the fracture be more, and the further away from the fracture, the less the tightening.
And the threads should be medium in thickness and thinness, and let them be of wet linen especially, because if the threads are thick, like what I have seen from the actions of the ignorant who make their threads from twisted linen strips, which is a grave mistake, because the tightening with them becomes excessive, and the thin threads are also not suitable because you will not achieve the tightening you desire with them.
And there should not be less than a finger's width between the splint and the splint, and if the patient is harmed by the edges of the splints after tightening in the correct places, then put under them soft lint or fluffed wool so that it does not harm him in any way.
And if the fracture is with a wound and a tear in the skin has occurred, we will mention it separately.
And know that not every fractured limb should be tightened with splints from the first day, because if the limb is large, the splints should not be placed on it except after five days or seven or more, depending on your safety from the occurrence of a hot swelling.
And this is the description of the bandages with which the ancients used to set fractures, which are placed on the fracture, the jaw, and the sprain.
Description of a brief, common bandage that sets fractures and is suitable for most temperaments, especially children and women, because it is neither predominantly hot nor cold.
And it is that you take from the dust of the millstone, which is the heart of the flour that clings to the walls of the millstone during the movement of the mill, then you knead it as it is, without sifting it, with egg white, and you make its kneading neither thick nor thin, then you use it.
Description of another bandage that sets fractures, dislocations, and sprains. يؤخذ من الماش واللادن والأقاقيا والراسن والمغاث والسكّ من كلّ واحد عشرة دراهم ومرّ وصبر من كلّ واحد خمسة دراهم ومن الأثل عشرون درهما ومن الطين الأرمنىّ او الرومىّ عشرون درهما يدقّ الجميع ويخلط بماء الأثل او ببياض البيض إن كان مزاج العليل محرورا ثمّ يستعمل هذا الضماد فإنّه حسن التأليف يجبر العظام المكسورة سريعا ويصلح لأكثر الناس لاعتداله،
Description of another bandage that is also useful for fractures and sprains. يؤخذ مغاث وماش وخطمىّ أبيض من كلّ واحد عشرة دراهم مرّ وصبر من كلّ واحد خمسة دراهم أقاقيا ستّة دراهم طين أرمنىّ عشرون درهما يدقّ الجميع دقّا ناعما وينخل ويعجن بالماء او ببياض البيض ويستعمل،
Description of a bandage for joints and bones displaced from their places, and it soothes the pain that occurs to them, and for bone fissures and fractures. يؤخذ الصوف الموذّح فيغمس فى الخلّ والزيت المطبوخ ويوضع على الموضع،
This bandage does not have the strength to set bones, but it is excellent in soothing hot swelling and relieving pains in particular.
Description of another bandage that sets broken bones. يؤخذ ورق التين الأصمّ وورق الخشخاش البرّىّ ويدقّا جميعا ويضمد بهما رطبين،
Description of another brief bandage used when the bone fracture is setting and you want to resolve the remaining swelling. تأخذ من أصل الخطمىّ والبابونج ونوار بنفسج ودقيق الكرسنّة من كلّ واحد جزءا دقّ الجميع واعجنه بالطلاء إن لم يكن العضو مستحرّا فإن كان مستحرّا فاعجنه بماء الكزبرة او بالماء واستعمله،
Description of another bandage, stronger in resolving than the first, used when a hard swelling occurs when the bone is setting. يؤخذ من أصل الخطمىّ وبزر الكتّان والحلبة وإكليل الملك ومرزنجوش ونوار بنفسج وبابونج من كلّ واحد جزء يدقّ الجميع ويعجن بماء الخلاف او بالماء العذب او بالطلاء كلّ ذلك على حسب حرارة العضو وسكون حرّه،
As for Hippocrates, he did not mention in his book that anything be placed on the fractured limb when setting it except the cerate made from wax and oil, nothing else, and he described it as being medium between thick and thin.
As for Galen, he saw that things that have dryness with some heat, such as myrrh, aloe, frankincense, and similar to what we mentioned, should be placed on the fractured limb when setting it.
Description of a bandage that is useful for weakness and pain. يؤخذ مغاث وحمّص وشعر إنسان مقروض او ريش طائر وخطمىّ وملح أجزاء سواء يدقّ وينخل ويعجن ويضمد به،
As for the amount of time the bandage should remain then be loosened, it is that you observe, and if the patient does not experience pain, itching, or movement of the broken bone from its place, then do not loosen it for many days.  If severe itching, disturbing pain, or swelling occurs in the area, then hasten to loosen it immediately and do not delay that at all, and remove the bandage from it.
Then take a soft cloth or a moist sea sponge, dip it in lukewarm water, and wash the area with it until the itching and pain subside. Then leave the limb to rest for an hour, then apply to it clarified wool dipped in vinegar and oil or rose oil, and tie it on for a night until you are safe from hot swelling, the swelling of the limb subsides, and its pain goes away.  Then return it to gentle tightening and a light bandage, and do not tighten it as your first tightening, and be gentle with it until it heals.
If you see that the swelling, redness, pain, swelling, and all the symptoms have completely disappeared, and you need the bandage and tightening, then reapply it as you did initially, exactly.
If none of what we mentioned occurs in the limb, do not loosen it except after three days or four or five or seven, and it may be left for twenty days, all of that depending on what you observe of the condition of the limb, as we said, until the fracture sets and the flesh nearly coagulates on it, then increase the tightening more than your first tightening altogether, and also increase the thickening of the patient's food as mentioned previously.
If you see that the site of the fracture has dried and become emaciated more than it should, then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it, so moisten it with lukewarm water every time you loosen it, and let that be every three days, and slightly reduce the tightening, for by this action nourishment will flow to the limb and it will heal quickly.
As for what the ignorant bone-setters do, of breaking the limb again if it did not set properly the first time and set crookedly, it is a mistake of their doing and a great deception. If it were correct, the ancients would have mentioned it in their books and practiced it, and I have not seen any trace of that from any of them at all, and the correct thing is not to do it.
The second chapter on the fracture occurring in the head.
The types of fractures occurring in the head are many, their forms are various, and their causes are diverse.
Some fractures result from a sword blow, and it may either completely shave the bone until it reaches the membrane beneath it, like an adze does to wood, and therefore this type of fracture is called adze-like (qadumi).
Or, the sword may cut part of the bone and shave only its surface without penetrating through it completely, and this type of fracture is called a simple extraction (qala).
The wound of these two types of fractures may be either large or small.
Some fractures are comminuted or crushed, caused by a blow with a stone or a fall on a stone or something similar.
This fracture is either penetrating, having almost reached the membrane under the bone, or it is on the surface of the bone. ويكون جرح هذين الكسرين أيضا إمّا كبيرا وإمّا صغيرا
Some fractures are hidden in the bone with the thinness of a hair, which is a slight fissure, and therefore this type of fracture is called hairline (sha'ri).
Among them is a fracture caused by a fall or a blow from a stone or something similar, which causes the surface of the bone to depress inward, creating a concavity, similar to what happens to a copper pot when struck, causing a part of it to depress inwards.
This most often occurs in heads with soft bones, like the heads of children.
All of these types of fractures may have loose or non-loose fragments. وسنأتى بذكر علاج ذلك كلّه فى موضعه
We identify all of these types of fractures by examining them, probing them with probes, and removing the dead flesh from them.
As for the hairline type, it is identified by exposing the bone, wiping it, and smearing it with ink; the fracture will appear black.
As for the treatment of fractures, first look at the patient's symptoms. If you see symptoms that clearly indicate danger, such as vomiting bile, stretching, loss of consciousness, loss of voice, fainting, acute fever, bulging and redness of the eyes, and other similar symptoms, then do not approach the patient nor treat him, for death is inevitable in most cases with these symptoms.
If you see symptoms that do not alarm you, and you hope for his recovery, then proceed with his treatment.
That is, if the injured person comes to you immediately after being injured, and it is during the winter, you should strive to remove the bone before the fourteenth day in any case.
If it is during the summer, you should hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before the membrane under the bone becomes infected, leading to the symptoms we described.
If the bone fracture has reached the membrane covering the brain, and it is accompanied by comminution and crushing, then you should cut the comminuted and crushed part as I will describe to you.
That is, shave the injured patient's head and expose the bone in any way possible, depending on the shape of the wound and what is easiest for the patient.
If you encounter bleeding or a hot swelling when exposing the bone, address it as necessary. وهو أن تحشو الموضع بخرق مغموسة فى شراب ودهن ورد ونقّ الجرح حتّى يسكن الورم وتأمن النزف
Then proceed to excise and remove the bone, which can be done in one of two ways.
The first way is to cut the bone with a fine, narrow-bladed instrument, and this is its image:
Then you use another piece after this piece, slightly wider, and this is its picture:
Then you also use another chisel, wider than the second.
And it is necessary that you have a set of different chisels, some of which are wider than others and some of which are shorter than others, and they should be extremely sharp at their edges. ولتكن من حديد هندىّ او فولاد جيّد.
And use gentleness in striking the chisel, lest you shake the head and harm it.
If the bone is strong and hard, then it is appropriate that you drill around it before using the chisels with the drills they called non-sinking drills.
And they called them non-sinking drills because they do not go beyond the limit of the skull bone to what is behind it, because the drill has a round edge below its sharp head, similar to a collar or small circle, that prevents it from sinking and exceeding the thickness of the bone.
And it is appropriate for you to take from these drills a large number, each one of which is suitable for the thickness of that bone, so that you have for each skull a drill whose sharp tip is of a length and shortness appropriate to the thickness of that skull.
And this is a picture of three types of drills: large, medium, and small:
As for the method of drilling around the broken bone, it is that you place the drill on the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated. ثمّ تنقل المثقب الى موضع آخر وتجعل بعد ما بين كلّ ثقب قدر غلظ المرود او نحوه،
Then you cut with the cutters between each two holes and do that with the utmost gentleness you can, as we said, until you remove the bone either with your hand or with something else from some of the instruments that were prepared for that, such as forceps and delicate hooks.
And you should be very careful that the drill or the cutter does not touch any of the periosteum.
So if you hollow out the bone and it is cleared of the periosteum, if it was attached to it, and you take it out, then you should scrape and smooth the roughness of what remains in the bone with another instrument similar to the cutter, except that it should be thinner and smoother than the rest of the cutters.
If anything remains of the small bones and fragments, then take them gently with the instruments you have, then treat the wound after that with bandages and ointments which will be mentioned.
As for the other aspect of the procedure, it is an easy aspect, far from danger, mentioned by Galen and praised highly, and this is his saying: ينبغى لك أوّلا أن تبدأ بكشف جزء العظم من الموضع الذى الكسر فيه أشدّ وأشهر حتّى اذا كشفت ذلك الجزء صيّرت تحته طرف هذا المقطع العدسىّ وهذه صورته:
The lenticular part of it should be smooth, not cutting anything, and the sharp part of it on its sides going lengthwise, as you see, so that the lenticular part rests on the peritoneum and the face of the sharp section is on the bone.
Then you strike the section from one side with a small hammer until you cut all the bone gently as it rotates, and you are safe from the membrane, no frightening accident will happen at all, even if the therapist is the most ignorant and cowardly of people, yes, even if he is sleepy.
If something attached to the bone from the membrane remains in some places of the bone, then scrape it off with the edge of the lenticular section itself and gently remove it from it, for it will be removed without harm or fear.
But if the bone fracture did not penetrate to the membrane, and what was cut from the bone was its surface, and roughness and fine fragments remained in it, then it is necessary to strip that roughness and remove those fragments with fine scrapers, of which several different sizes have been made, so that you can use them in every place that is most suitable and most appropriate, depending on what the work and the broken bone itself and its shape lead you to.
And you should use in your stripping first for the bone the widest of those scrapers, then use after it a finer one, and you continue to do that successively until you get to using the finest and thinnest of them all.
As for the rest of the small capillary cracks and the fine fracture, it is necessary to use a treatment for each one according to what leads to its healing, and it is something that is not hidden from anyone who has the slightest training in this craft and has come across what we have written and explained in major fractures.
If the membrane remains exposed when you remove the bone, then you should take a linen cloth the size of the wound and dip it in rose oil and place it on the mouth of the wound, then take another folded or triangular cloth and dip it in syrup and rose oil and place it on the first cloth, and do that as lightly as you can so that you do not weigh down the peritoneum, then use a wide bandage over it and do not tighten it except as much as it holds the wound only.
And you leave it with this treatment for a day or two until you are safe from the hot swelling, then you dissolve it and use after that the medicines that have excess dryness, such as orris roots, cress flour, frankincense powder, and zedoary, and the like, which is to make a powder from these medicines and sprinkle it on the wound as it is dry, and in general, use a medicine in it that clarifies and does not sting.
And strive your best while treating the wound that it be clean, that there be no harm to the fat or dirt in it, and do not let pus gather in it at all, because pus, if it gathers on the brain membrane, corrupts it and rots it, so a great affliction happens to the patient from that.
Blackness may appear on the surface of the peritoneum of the head when the bone is exposed from it, especially if its treatment is neglected, you have observed. If the symptoms we mentioned appear to the patient, then know that he is inevitably doomed.
And if the blackness only occurred from a medicine placed on it, and it was in the power of that medicine to cause that blackness, then it is necessary to take one part of honey and three parts of rose oil and beat them well and smear a cloth with them, then place it on the peritoneum, then treat it with the types of treatment that are necessary until it heals.
The third chapter on the reduction of the nose if it is broken.
Know that nothing breaks from the nose except its upper two sides together or one of them, because they are bones, because the lower part of it is cartilage and does not break.
But rather, it is subject to bruising, bending, and flattening.
If one of its sides breaks, then you should insert the little finger into the nostril and straighten that fracture from the inside with your index finger and thumb from the outside until you restore the nose to its natural shape. وليكن ذلك منك برفق وتتحرّى أن لا يحدث بفعلك ذلك على العليل وجع،
If the fracture is in the top of the nose and the finger cannot reach it, then it should be straightened with the tip of a slightly thick probe.
If the fracture is on both sides, then do the same.
And hasten to set it on the first day of the fracture if possible, otherwise after the seventh or tenth day when the hot swelling subsides.
Then insert into the nostril a wick of linen scraps if the fracture is on one side, or insert two wicks if the fracture is on both sides. ولتكُن الفتل فيها غلظ على قدر ما يملا ثقب الأنف،
Some of the early bone-setters mentioned that the wicks should be moistened with fat and changed every day. ولست أرى أنا ذلك
You may insert goose feather tubes in the nose in place of the wicks after wrapping soft rags around them. فيكون حبسها لكسر الأنف أشدّ ولئلا يمتنع العليل من التنفّس
This is not necessary, if you wish, do it, and if you wish, make the wicks.
If a hot swelling occurs in the nose during your work, then dress the nose with cerate or with cotton dipped in vinegar and rose oil or some of the diachylon ointment.
If a hot swelling does not occur, then you should dress it from the outside with semolina flour and frankincense powder mixed with egg white. ثمّ تضع عليه مشاقة ليّنة ولا تربط الأنف بشىء البتّة،
If the nasal bones are broken into small pieces or shattered, then you should incise over them and remove them with the appropriate instrument. ثمّ تخيط الشقّ وتعالجه بما يلحم ويدمل من المراهم الموافقة لذلك،
If a wound occurs inside the nose, then you should treat it with wicks and use lead tubes until it heals.
Chapter four on the reduction of the lower jaw if it is broken
If the lower jaw is fractured and the fracture is not accompanied by a wound, examine it. If the fracture is external only and not a double fracture and it is concave inward, then its diagnosis is easy. Thus, if the fracture is in the right fissure, you should insert the index finger of the left hand into the patient's mouth. Similarly, if the fracture is in the left fissure, you should insert the index finger of the right hand.  Gently lift the hump of the fracture outward from the inside, and with your other hand from outside the bone, control and align it.
If the fracture of the jaw is in two pieces, then extension should be applied from both sides in a straight line until you can align it.
If the teeth have become loose or separated, tie those that you hope will remain with gold, silver, or silk thread. Then, place cerate on the broken jaw, and place a folded cloth over it. On the cloth, place a large, tight splint or a piece of shoe leather equal to the length of the jaw. Then bandage it from above as it seems appropriate to you to bandage it and tie it so that it will not become loose. Order the patient to be quiet and still, and make his food soft broths.
If you think that something has changed in the shape in any way, then quickly undo it on the third day. Then repair what you bandaged with mill dust mixed with egg white or with semolina flour, after removing the cerate from it. Place a soft bandage on the dressing.
As long as that dressing adheres to it and the condition of the bone has not changed, leave it and do not undo it until it heals and the fracture becomes firm. This fracture often becomes firm in three weeks.
If a hot swelling occurs during that time, use what we have mentioned repeatedly to soothe it until the swelling subsides.
However, if the fracture is accompanied by a wound, examine it. If a fragment or fragments have separated from the bone, be gentle in extracting those fragments with whatever instrument you find appropriate.
If the mouth of the wound is narrow, widen it with a scalpel as much as you need.
Then, when you have extracted those fragments and nothing of them remains, stitch the mouth of the wound if it is wide. Otherwise, apply one of the ointments suitable for it and join the wound until it heals.
The fifth chapter on the reduction of the clavicle if it is fractured
The clavicle is most often fractured from the front at the end of the shoulder.
Its fracture takes one of three forms: either it breaks and splits in two without producing fragments, which is the easiest to reduce; or fragments occur in the fracture, which is more difficult to reduce; or the fracture is accompanied by a wound.
The procedure in it, if the fracture is without a wound, is to bring two assistants, one of whom stabilizes the arm next to the fractured clavicle and the other extends the neck toward the other side.
Then you straighten the fracture with your fingers until its shape becomes as it should be, and there is no protrusion or curvature in it.
If you need more extension, it is appropriate to place a ball of rags or wool under the patient's armpit, its size being according to your need, and you extend and raise the clavicle and press the ball with your hand until the fracture is straightened as it should be.
If you are unable to pull the end of the clavicle outward because it has gone deep, it is appropriate for the patient to lie on his back and a medium-sized pillow be placed under his shoulder, and the assistant presses his shoulder downward until the clavicle bone that is deep rises up, then at that time, fix the fracture and straighten it with your fingers
If you feel that a fragment of the clavicle has broken and has started to move, it is appropriate to make an incision over it and remove that fragment gently.
If the fragment is trapped in the bone, be clever in cutting it with one of the cutters that you have prepared for this purpose, after the instrument that protects the peritoneum, which is an instrument made of wood or iron, has been placed under the clavicle, and this is its image:
It resembles a spoon without a curvature, its width depending on the size of the bone you need, and its length depending also on what allows you to work, and let it have two ends, as you see, one end wider and the other narrower.
If the tear in the wound that you made when removing the bone fragment is wide, and you are safe from a hot swelling, bring together the lips of the wound with stitches.
And if the tear is small, or you fear a hot swelling, pack the wound with bandages and pads according to the extent of the wound's opening.
If a hot swelling appears, soak the bandage in rose oil and vinegar or wine, and apply it on it.
As for bone setting, if it is without a wound or incision, it is that you apply on the bone the poultice made from mill dust with egg white, and place the soft compress on it, then place the ball under his armpit if needed. ثمّ تأخذ عمامة تكون طويلة جدّا ويكون عرضها شبرا او نحو ذلك
And the key to it all is that the splint does not move from the broken bone, and therefore it is necessary that you check the patient every day, so whenever the bandage loosens and you see that the splint has moved, fix that and tighten the bandage.
Then make the patient sleep on his back and put a small pillow under his armpit when he sleeps at night so that his arm is raised from his side, so the clavicle fracture is raised by raising the shoulder, or tie his arm to his neck.
And do not loosen the bandage unless there is an incident in the area, such as itching or swelling, until twelve days.
Then renew the poultice if you see reason for that, and repeat the tightening, and leave it until the clavicle fracture heals and sets.
And it becomes stronger and firmer than that in twenty-eight days, and it may be in some people in less than that.
Chapter Six on the reduction of shoulder fractures.
The shoulder blade rarely breaks in its wide part. وإنّما تنكسر منها حروفها
Whenever a place in it breaks, or it breaks in its middle, which is known by touch, depending on the shape of the fracture, you must straighten it and return it to its natural shape by any means possible.
Then apply to the area mill dust with egg white and soft mashaqa (a type of plaster). وضع من فوق رفادة من خرقة مثنيّة
If there is a curvature under the splint in any place of the shoulder, correct that curvature with soft mashaqa until the splint sits evenly.
Then tighten it from above with a long turban, tightly and firmly, and secure the splint so that it does not move from its place.
Check the bandage every day; whenever the bandage loosens, tighten it and adjust the splint if it has moved from its place.
The patient should lie on his healthy side.
The shoulder heals in twenty or twenty-five days.
When this period is complete, untie the bandage and you will be safe, for it is among the bones that are not feared to shatter or deteriorate.
If a splinter of bone protrudes and it pricks under the skin, incise it and extract it, and do what I mentioned in the fracture of the clavicle about soothing the hot swelling if any of that occurs.
Chapter seven on the treatment of rib fractures.
The chest may be fractured in its middle, and this rarely happens. وأمّا أطرافه فهى أكثر ما تنكسر وتنفتّت
And among its symptoms, if its middle is fractured, is that it inclines downwards, and he experiences severe pain, difficulty breathing, coughing, and perhaps he spits up blood, and the concavity in the broken bone becomes apparent and is not hidden to the senses.
Its treatment is that the patient lies on his back, and a pillow is placed between his shoulders, then his shoulders are pressed, and the ribs are gathered with the hands from both sides, and gentleness is exercised in straightening it in every way possible and suitable until the shape of the bone returns as it should be.
Then apply the dressing and the binding, and place over it a splint from a thin board of willow or heather, or something similar in lightness, after wrapping it in a cloth. ثمّ تلطّف فى ربطها على العظم المكسور لئلّا تزول
And if the need arises to loosen it, due to itching that occurs in the area, or pain, or swelling, then hasten to loosen it, remove the dressing, and treat whatever has occurred in its proper manner, then replace the dressing if you see a reason for it, and keep it tight until it heals.
The eighth chapter on the mending of ribs if they are broken.
Know that rib fractures occur only in the thick parts adjacent to the back. وأطرافها من قدّام إنّما يعرض لها الرضّ من أجل أنّها غضروفيّة
Its reduction is by straightening the fracture with the fingers in the most effective way until the shape is as it should be. ثمّ تضمّد وتشدّ العظم المكسور بجبيرة إن احتاج الى ذلك
If the rib fracture is tilted inwards, it causes the patient severe pain and a stabbing sensation like the stabbing that occurs to someone with pleurisy, because the bone stabs the diaphragm. ويعرض له أيضا عسر النفس والسعال وقذف دم كثير وهذا عسر العلاج
The ancients devised many tricks for it.
Some of them said it is necessary to make the patient's food something that generates flatulence and wind so that the abdomen swells and expands and pushes the fracture outwards.
We dislike this lest it confirms the occurrence of a hot swelling if it has not occurred. فإن كان قد حدث فإنّه يزيد فيه ويؤكّده
Some of them said a cupping glass is placed on the area and then sucked strongly, and this is more like the standard, except that it is feared that the cupping glass will attract superfluities to the area because of its weakness.
Some of them said the area should be covered with wool dipped in hot oil, and padding should be placed between the ribs until it is filled, so that the bandage is level when you wrap it around circularly.
Then treat the patient with the treatment of pleurisy in terms of food and medicine.
If the patient is exhausted by a severe matter that he cannot bear and the bone stabs the diaphragm with a harmful stab, and we fear for the patient, then we should incise the area and expose the broken rib. ثمّ نصير تحته الآلة التى تحفظ الصفاق التى تقدّم وصفها ونقطع العظم برفق ونخرجه
Then we bring together the edges of the wound, if it is large, with sutures and treat it with ointments until it heals.
If a hot swelling occurs during that, immediately wet padding in rose oil and place it on the area. وعالج العليل بما يسكن الورم من داخل أيضا ويستلقى على الجانب الذى يخفّ عليه النوم حتّى يبرأ
The ninth chapter on the mending of the vertebrae of the back and neck.
As for the bones of the neck, if a fracture occurs in them – and this rarely happens to them, what happens most often is a bruise, and likewise the vertebrae of the back as well – if that happens to someone and you want to know whether he will recover or not, look: if you see that his hands have become limp, numb, and dead, and he is unable to move them, or stretch them, or grasp with them, and if you pinch them or prick them with a needle, he does not feel it and does not find any pain in them, then know that he will not recover in most cases, he is doomed.
And if he moves them and feels the pinch and the pricking in them, then know that the bone marrow is safe and that the patient will recover with treatment.
If something similar happens to the vertebrae of the back, and you want to know whether he will also recover or not, look at his legs.  If you see that they have become limp and what happened to the hands has also happened to them, then if he lies on his back, wind and stool come out involuntarily, and if he lies on his stomach, urine comes out involuntarily, and if he lies on his back and wants to urinate, he is unable to do so, then know that he is doomed, so do not bother with his treatment.
If none of this happens to him, the matter is less severe.
And the treatment for what happened is that you try to soothe the hot swelling by placing rose oil alone, or with roasted egg whites, on the bruised vertebra.  Apply this three times a day until the hot swelling subsides. Then apply to the area one of the strengthening, drying bandages and tighten it with a bandage. Order him to rest and stay still, and not to sleep except on the side where he feels no pain until he recovers.
If a splinter or something has occurred in the bone during the bruise, from which pus has come out, then it is necessary to cut the skin over it and extract that bone. Then bring the edges of the wound together, if it is large, with stitching, then treat it with healing ointments until it recovers.
If the last bone of the coccyx, which is the tailbone, is broken, it is necessary to insert the thumb of the left hand into the anus and straighten the broken bone with the other hand as much as possible and as straightening allows. Then apply a bandage and a splint if needed, and then tighten it.
If you feel a broken splinter in it, cut over it and extract it, and treat the wound with the treatment mentioned previously until it heals.
The tenth chapter on the reduction of hip fracture.
Rarely do the hip bones break. فإن انكسرت فإنّما يكون كسرها أن تتفتّت فى أطرافها وتنشقّ فى الطول وتميل الى داخل
Its treatment is to run your hand over it until you determine the shape of the fracture.
If the break is only at the ends, then adjust the fracture according to what you can achieve in setting it until its shape resembles its natural shape.
If the break is lengthwise, or if it has tilted inward, then lay the patient on his stomach so that you can set the fracture. فإذا سوّيته حملت عليه الضماد ثمّ تضع عليه جبيرة من خشب او من جلد وشدّه شدّا لا تخاف عليه انتقال الكسر ولا زوال الجبيرة
And level the hollow of the flanks with whatever fills it so that the tightening takes hold evenly. وتأمر العليل أن ينام على ظهره او على جنبه الصحيح
If a hot swelling appears, stop extending and setting it until the hot swelling subsides, and apply what soothes it, as described previously, then return to setting it and tightening it as appropriate.
If fragments appear in the bone, or if anything shatters from its ends, it should not be removed or touched, but rather leveled from the outside, as we have said, and left untightened until it heals.
Chapter eleven on the reduction of a fracture of the humerus
The upper arm is what is between the elbow and the head of the shoulder.
If it breaks, then set it in one of two ways.
One of them is that you take a curved, smooth stick of medium thickness in this way:
And you tie two bandages at its ends, then hang it from a high place, and the patient sits on a chair, then his broken arm is placed on the wood until his armpit is attached to the middle of the curve of the wood, then a heavy object is hung from above it or an assistant stretches it downwards, then the physician adjusts the fracture with both his hands until the fracture returns to what it should be.
The other way is for the patient to lie on his back and his hand is hung from his neck with a bandage, then you order two assistants, one of them to hold what is above the fracture with his hands, and the other to hold what is below it and each of them stretches it to his side.
And if you want the stretching to be stronger, tighten below the fracture with a bandage and above it with a bandage, and each of the assistants stretches them to their side.
If the fracture is close to the end of the shoulder, then the [middle] of the bandage should be under the armpit, and the other under the fracture towards the elbow.
Similarly, if the fracture is close to the elbow, then the bandage should be placed on that place and on the elbow itself.
Then adjust the fracture gently without violence until the fracture is aligned as it should be and heals well, then tighten it if no hot swelling appears, but if a hot swelling appears, leave it tightened until the seventh day, and put on it clarified wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil until the swelling subsides, then tighten it.
The method of tightening it is to apply the dressing to the fracture, then apply a roll of new cloth over the dressing, then gather the forearm onto the upper arm itself and place his hand open on his shoulder.
And apply the cloth and the tightening on the upper arm and forearm so that the forearm acts as splints, if nothing prevents you from doing so and nothing has changed on the broken bone.
If you fear that something might change on you from that, then use splints, which is to place on the fracture itself a splint that is wider and stronger than the other splints, and let the width of a finger be between each splint, and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with an increase of three fingers from each side, then tighten on the splints the tightening that I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter, which is that your tightening on the site of the fracture should be stronger, and the further away the fracture is, the less the tightening should be.
If you see fit to place the splints and the tightening as we said while you are setting the limb from your hour, then do so.
And if you fear the hot swelling, then leave the tightening and the splints until the seventh day, as we said, then check the bandage every three days so that no itching or swelling occurs in the place, or that food is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive tightening, so fix all of that as we mentioned.
If you are confident that nothing of that will happen, then do not loosen the bandage except after many days.
And the patient should lie on his back with his hand on his stomach, and a moderate elbow pad filled with wool is placed under the upper arm.
And check at all times, night or day, so that the shape of the broken limb does not become distorted or the bandage loosens, so fix all of that with your effort.
And make the patient's food of the order that we presented, that the food be light at first until the bone starts to strengthen, then you should thicken his food.
It is customary for the upper arm and leg to strengthen in forty days, so then it should be loosened and the bath should be used and treated with ointments that are suitable for that.
If the fracture is severe and crushed, do not loosen the bandage from it until fifty days or two months.
The twelfth chapter on the reduction of a fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones called the two ulnas, one of which is small, and it is the one next to the thumb, and the other is large, and it is located below the small one from the bottom; so perhaps the larger ulna may be broken alone or the small one alone, and perhaps both may be broken together.
So when the small upper ulna is broken, its repair is easy and its healing is faster, and when the lower ulna is broken, its breakage is bad and its healing is difficult, and the worst of it is if the two bones break together.
So, if the bone that is broken is the small upper ulna, then the physician should, when repairing it, make its extension slight gently until he straightens it; and if the large ulna is the one that is broken, then he should make its extension stronger; and if both ulnas are the ones that are broken, then he should make the extension much stronger.
And it is necessary to place the shape of the hand when repairing it and extending it stretched out on a cushion, and the thumb of the hand should be upwards, higher than all the fingers, and the little finger should be lower than the rest of the fingers, and the patient sitting cross-legged, and let the cushion be opposite him in height so that the patient is not burdened with hardship; then a servant extends the arm from below, either with his hand or with a bandage, and another servant extends from above likewise; then the physician straightens the bone until he restores it to the best shape he can.
And if there are splinters in the bone fracture, then you try to return each splinter to its place with your best effort. If a loose splinter appears in it and it is piercing the skin, and you have no hope of repairing it, then incise upon it and extract it in the manner we mentioned previously.
And if the fracture is with a wound, then I have devoted a chapter to it, so you take the treatment for that from there.
And if a hot swelling occurs at the beginning of your repair, then smear a cloth with the cerate made with rose oil and white wax, and let it be medium between thick and thin, and tie the cloth on it with a gentle tie until the swelling subsides, then remove the cerate and apply the poultice prepared from mill dust with egg white, then apply the splints, and let the splint that is placed on the fracture itself be slightly wider and stronger.
And know that the number of arm splints is six in most cases, whether the fracture is in one ulna or in both ulnas together.
Then make your tightening on the site of the fracture stronger and tighter, and whenever you turn the tightening upwards or downwards, make the tightening slightly looser, as mentioned previously at the beginning of the chapter.
And let the cloths that are wrapped on the fracture be soft and moist cloths, and let them not be very hard, and let the thread that is tied with it be specifically linen, medium between thin and thick, as we described.
And examine the limb and the bandage after a few days, and if something happens that needs to be corrected, such as an itch occurring in the limb, then it is necessary to wash the limb with warm water until the itch subsides, and leave the limb untied for a night until it rests, then resume tightening.
And if the tightening has loosened and the bone has shifted or something like that, then repair all of that with your best effort.
And look also, and if the nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive tightening, then you should loosen it a little and leave it for a few days until the nourishment flows to it, then tighten it.
And if nothing of what we mentioned happens to the patient, then it should not be loosened until after twenty days or so.
Then hang the patient's hand from his neck, and let his arm be straight, and let him diligently avoid turbulent movements, and let his sleep be on his back.
And know that this fracture of the arm heals in thirty days or in thirty-two, and perhaps it heals in twenty-eight days, all of that according to the states of the temperaments and the states of strength.
Chapter thirteen on the treatment of fractures of the palm and fingers.
The metacarpus and phalanges of the fingers rarely suffer fractures, but they are frequently subject to contusions.
When a fracture or contusion occurs in the hand, the patient should sit cross-legged with a chair of equal height in front of him, then place his outstretched hand on it. Then, an assistant should extend the broken bones, and the physician should align them until they are well-joined. At that point, a bandage and a splint should be applied if there is no hot swelling.
Then a splint, the size of the affected area, should be applied on top, having been inserted into a soft cloth.
If the fracture is downwards towards the palm of the hand, make something like a ball from a piece of cloth and instruct the patient to grasp it with his fractured hand.  Then, tie it with a long cloth, and let the splint be made of supple leather so that the leather conforms to the flexion of the entire hand, and tighten it as needed.
If the fracture is outwards, you should apply a splint from above and another splint from below in the hand so that the hand remains open and straight. Then apply the bandaging as the hand turns and connect between the fingers with the bandage.
If the fracture affects one of the phalanges of the fingers, if it is the thumb, it should be aligned as necessary, then bandaged with the hand.
